“THE BULLETIN” BOOK EXCHANGE.
On receipt at The Bulletin Office, Sydney, of Postal Order, Postal Notes, or Stamps (of any province) equal to the price stated
(which is published price with postage added), books in the subjoined list will be forwarded to any Australasian address.
A NEW BOOK.
“The Gods, Some Mortals, and Lord Wicken-
ham,” by John Oliver Hobbes (cloth, 6s. 6d.),
oompanions the author’s previously-collected
works in size and price (Some Emotions and a
Moral; The Sinner’s Comedy; A Study in
Temptations ; A Bundle of Life ; with portrait—
cloth, 6s. 6d.). The uniformity might have ex-
tended to binding also, in spite of the change of
publishers.
Mrs. Craigie’s new book has keen pathos, for it
is the book of an unhappy woman. One did not
need the confirmation of her divorce to diagnose
a bruised heart behind the brilliancy. Such pearls
are for her sex, alas ! an abnormal secretion —they
mean a life-current turned awry. No wonder
Mrs. Craigie’s epigrams dazzle ; they are written
with her biood.
The mood of most of the previous stories was
that of satire sown with sobs; now the sobs are
stifled, and the satire is sadder for their suppres-
sion. The author’s perception and philosophy
seem deeper, calmer; the phrase-making is hardly
so clever as before, but the stream flows more
strongly beneath the rarer bubbles of bright foam.
Does the story reflect Mrs. Oraigie’s own life ?
Was she the one who lost all to marry a wanton
at the instigation of impetuous Sex ?
The talent and most of the interest of the book
are in the first two-thirds; the remainder adds a
comparatively dull inevitable.
The old Faust-contest—far older than Faust
contest—between flesh and spirit is again shadowed
in Simon Warre, a medical specialist who has
spent the sap of his youth in study which brought
fame and wealth, but not happiness. At 27, he
already felt that need for the society of a com-
plaisaiUe woman which Yoltaire attributes to nearly
*n men past leur premiere jeuncsse. There is a
strife between higher and lower nature, cerebrum
and cerebellum, divine love and human lust. He
yearns to Allegra, a shy, slender child, who
“ seemed to have been whipped up into a fragile
existence from the very cream of tenderness,
weakness, love, and folly.” He marries, hating
himself, Anne, a superb animal, whom her lover’s
death shocks into wedding-day recital of a sordid,
Jitiable past. Warre’s character has a weak
;rain; there is no separation; gradually he drains
his cup to the dregs. There is an ineffectual
meeting with Allegra; Anne flees with a rich
admiper; Warre dies of heart-break. Allegra
gives her ashes' to Lord Wiekenham, Warre’s
friend, whose occasional appearance in the back-
ground supplies justification for his name’s inclu-
sion in the book’s fantastic title.
The feature of the book is the character of Anne,
whom it would be curious aud profitable to com-
pare and contrast with Beatrix aud Madame
Bovary. She is a strongly-drawn type of the sen-
sual Eve; Allegra a more weakly-suggested por-
trait of the spiritual. The author rightly insists
on their feminine identity. “ Allegra was an
innocent Anne; Anne was a vicious Allegra.”
In strenuous passage after passage Anne is por-
trayed as only woman—or genius—can portray
woman. Wane is not nearly so well done: his
attitudes and actions are always unconvincing.
The rest is brilliant sketching only; and the book
as a whole, in spite of its extreme cleverness, is
episodal and temporary.
Incidentally the author seems to say how little
men know women. Here is Allegra’s affection
concealed under a mask of stiffness, and Warre
does not guess, or is never sure. But would most
men of intellect, imen of experience, not see the
truth in the circumstances ?
Apparently Mrs. Craigie still clings to religion:
it is only when she is given a good man that
woman can dispense with the good God. How often
feminine derelicts seek from a priest the consola-
tion which they have failed to find in a husband !
Some good things:
“He was a dear, but dull; good-looking in a
way, but too much lake a stone Roman in a photo-
graph of ruins.”
“Why is there ho Eden for some Eves ? Why
is it that only God is good enough and the devil
is bad enough to be safe in their company ? ”
“ All women are unladylike when they get in a
temper; they remember all the naughty things
men have told thorn.”
“She is a good little woman with a rather
at tractive squint. And so kind to the poor. ”
“ Have you ever seen him on a horse i He looks
like the last match left in a tray—the one which
won’t burn.”
“You see, it was hopeless from the beginning;
so he told me he loved me, and I told him I loved
him, and we kissed each other good-bye. That
was all that happened. It is a very short story to
tell, but it’s my whole life, Anne. All the rest is
merely time time just hours, weeks, and
months.” . .
“ You want a bourgeois devotion expressed in
the spirit of Dante.” [A characteristio male
want V] . .
“ Hers was an inquisitiveness, too, which was
as far removed from sympathy as old straw is from
new-mown hay. If a friend broke his leg she
would ask him in how many places, and whether
he groaned.” .
“ Sir Hugh Delaware was a baronet in reduced
circumstances who, in the early part of his career,
had spent his small fortune in riotous excess,
driven fast horses, and been driven by a fast lady.
This person, whom he could never be induced to
abuse, was known as his ‘ Ruin; ’ and Lady
Delaware, whom he had never been heard to
praise, was called his ‘Salvation.’”
“ She owned that she was not at all clever, and
did not understand social questions. She only
wished that spotted veils would go out of fashion.
They hurt her eyes.”
A suggestion of Anne :
“ As Anne drove away, she thought how com-
fortable Warre’s house was, how agreeable it would
be to live there, and how much she loved him. She
did not consider him handsome ; his good looks
were of a type which she did not understand. She
liked something more conventional —more effec-
tive, in fact. But he was very nice, and what
lovely Persian carpets ! What fine pictures on the
staircases 1 He surely made a lot of money—and
he was no doubt generous. The servant looked
contented. Oh, Simon was charming! She lewd
him dearly. She hoped the drawing-room was
large. All the interesting people in London would
come and see them. She would have a grand
wedding, for, of • course, he meant to marry her.
He had told her to break off with the other'brute.
And the other was a brute. She hated him. But
he bad given her some beautiful presents. Would
she be obliged to send them book ? ”
This pinning-down of specimens is of course
brutally unfair to the author, whose book should
be bought and read twice through. But is not
the last bit worthy of Thackeray ?
PUBLICATIONS.
“ Peter Steele, the Cricketer,” by H. 6. Hut-
chinson (Macmillan’s Colonial Library; paper,
2s. IDd.; cloth, 3s. lOd.) is an unpretentious
romance of love and cricket from which Australian
willow-patrons should extract a great deal of
pleasure.
Three really good Australian publications are
Cole’s “Fun Doctor” and the first and second
series of Cole’s “Thousand Best Poems” (each —
paper, 25.; cloth, 35.) The first is about the best
collection of humorous matter extant at the price;
and the. others bring together in handy form a
very wide choice of popular verses, classical pieces
being intermingled with many of those stirring bits
of sentiment which go straight to the heart and
stick there.
“Light as the Interpretation of the Law of
Gravity,” by A. M. Cameron (Angus and Robert-
son), is mere midwinter madness.
“ The Time Machine,” by H. G. Wells (paper,
Is. 9d.) is a very clever and interesting tale of
humanity’s future —one of the best fancies of the
kind ever composed.
In “The Australian at Home,” by Edward King-
lake (cloth, 35.), are collected the sanest criticisms
of Australian life anywhere to be met. The
book has not, of course, the fascination and
literary strength of Francis Adams’ “ Austra-
lians,” but it is sensible, proportioned, and true.
Most of the im. Aer is certainly famdiar, not to say
stale ; but this objection does not apply in the case
of the English reader —who gets these “useful
hints ” among others offered “to those intending
to settle in Australia ” :
“ti. As you won’t want to shoot anybody
(except perhaps yourself), you needn’t buy a
revolver. Many other articles will realise more at
the pawnshop.
“15. If you are a ‘bad lot,’ and the dear old
folks at home want to get rid of you by packing
you off to the other side of the world, add to your
iniquities by refusing to come. Australia doesn’t
want you.”
Two new volumes in the “ Social Science Series”
(cloth, 3s. each) are “ Work and Wages,” by
Thorold Rogers, being a reprint of the most im-
portant chapters from the professor’s larger work ;
and Rousseau’s famous “ Social Contract,” with a
lengthy and valuable introduction. Social students
will eagerly welcome these —two of the best books
in a good and cheap series.
“The Humor of America” (paper, 3s. ; cloth,
45.) is the best volume of the “ Library of Humor”
series. The writers are not cramped in trans-
lations, but sport freely on their native heath.
The selections are good, new, and modern ; there
are many illustrations; and a biographical index
is attached. Decidedly a book to buy.
“The Sexes Compared, etc.” (cloth, 35.), is a
translation of several essays by von Hartmann,
the German philosopher who has perhaps more
kinship with Schopenhauer than with any other
predecessor or contemporary. Hartmann w read-
able and suggestive ; and the argument of the first
essay is worth consideration by the New Woman.
The feature of the handsome Illustrated
London News Summer Number (Is. 3d.) is a new
story,“ Toxin,” by Uuida.
Lovers of Gordon wdl welcome the graphic
illustrations of himself and his poems issued by
the Somerset Publishing Co. Under the title of
“ Reminiscences and Unpublished Poems ” (cloth,
Bs. (id.) are given “ Argemone” (recently published
in The Bulletin), some characteristic “Verses to
my Old Black Pipe,’’and an equally characteristic
letter. Three clever drawings illustrate Gordon’s
grave, the leap over the fence at Mt. Gambler, and
Gordon reciting in camp. The last is specially
good, and all are well-reproduced on plate paper
12ui. x The same publishers issue six illus-
trations of Gordon’s poem “From the Wreck,”
suitable for framing, on mounts 20in. x lOin.
(10s. (id. the set). The artist, who prefers to be
anonymous, has produced some uncommonly strik-
ing pictures, full of energy and truth, and well
worth a place on Australian walls.
“ The Speed Secret, a short cut to rapid work
in Shorthand and Typewriting” (9d.) should be
bought by every typist and shorthander.
Anyone who has not made the acquaintance of
that pleasant woman poet, Jean Ingelow, can do
so with satisfaction in the admirable selection of
her “Lyrical and Other Poems” (cloth, 35.) just
published.
NOTES.
The sale of “ Pickwick Papers ” is now close on
700,000.
Herbert Spencer recently declined a German
knighthood, on the ground that rational philo-
sophy and titles don’t chime. Yet London
Chronicle, the leading democratic daily, remarks,
with apparent depreciation, that Higinbotham
was “ nothing more ” than plain Mr. to the end.
An American literary critic says Nordau’s
mare’s-nests lie thick as leaves in Lombroso.
Max Pemberton, the clever writer of Jules
Vernish romances, before writing “The Sea
Wolves ” made a careful study of the modes of
transporting gold from England to Russia, and in
his story worked out a scheme by which an
immense amount of bullion was stolen in course of
transit from the tugs to the steamers. Lend.
Spectator says that certain firms of English
financiers noted the story, and held enquiry to see
whether the scheme was feasible. They found it
was, and as a result the methods of transport have
been changed entirely, and the use of tugs has
been discontinued.
Though his name still appears as editoi-, Jerome
is said to have sold for .£20,000 his paper, To-Dav,
which signalised itself by offering an advertising
silver medal to a young Australian who saved his
brother from a shark. Under Jerome, To-Day has
been generally readable and invariably snobbish.
It started with some good illustrations and original
articles, but latterly the illustrations have grown
scarce and the editorial scissors have risen every
morning at 5.30. Jerome will now presumably
devote himself exclusively to the creation of
“new humor.” He is a pushing young man, and,
if he were not so bumptious, would be amusing.
As it is, he is exceedingly amusing.
A London publisher, Andrew Tuer, tells the
Athrn.'EUM of “another terror.” “In the open-
ing page to some typed sample chapters sent over
here by a colonial authoress for English publishers
to taste is the following refreshingly audacious
foot-note :— ‘ Where inverted commas have been
omitted, I desire to thank those authors whose
works I have quoted.’ ” Name, please !
Mrs. paffyn has written a short tale for “ The
Zeit-Geist Library,” entitled “ A Comedy in
Spasms. ”
NOTE.—As most of the books men-
tioned are in strong demand, some may
be temporarily out of stock. In such
case, orders will be filed, and the books
forwarded immediately on arrival of next
shipment.

O XT R S E L V B S !
We wish It to be understood that the character of the
business we do, and the absolutely inclusive variety of the
goods we sell, entitle us to be called
UNIVERSAL PROVIDERS.
We are also not far out, considering the low prices we
ask for everything we sell, and the consequent blessings
we are continually showering upon the people, when w'e
assert, not in timorous tones, but with the trumpet voice
of confidence in the truth of what we say, when we dub
ourselves
PUBLIC BENEFACTORS.
There are good men in every sphere of life —honest
citizens in every community, whose deeds stamp them
for the approval of their fellow-men, and single them out
from the common herd. Thus we find among the crowd
who aspire to serve their country on the benches of the
Legislative Assembly a patriot here and there who stands
out in bold relief against the majority, whose sole aim
is place and pay. And in commercial life the same thing
occurs, and the same strong contrast between the few
disinterested friends of the people, and the many selfish
lookers after the big profits they would like to make, is
plainly apparent. To the former class, to the little band
of merchants tvho study their customers’ interests, and
who, in self-abnegation and contentedness, are willing to
accept small profits in reward for their services, belong
ANTHONY HORDERN’S.
They say that a good action is its own reward, but if the
good action entail hard work, much tribulation, and con-
tinual strife, then surely the laborer is worthy of his hire,
and the doer of good actions entitled to some recompense
at the hands of those who profit by what he does.
ANTHONY HORDERN’S
never cease from their toil; in the burden and heat of the
noonday sun they work, oppressed by the cruel frost of
somewhile baffled efforts and frustrated negotiations, they
continue, knowing always that the approval of millions of
souls is theirs. For three-quarters of a century it has
been so, and for a longer time than that it will remain as
it has been. Generations passed away have bought from
the great house, and generations yet to come will do the
same. Like the practice of honesty or truth, there is a
policy in it which brings to its adherents
PROFIT AND SATISFACTION.
ANTHONY HORDERN & SONS,
UNIVKUSAL PKOVIBEUS.

COMMONPLACES.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Here I sit in fruitless fretting,
Anxious surmise, painful guess.
With a brain that fast is getting
Rank with fostered idleness ;
In a useless, aimless fashion
Let my thoughts arise and die,
Hopeless of a true expression,
Heedless how the minutes fly.
What is Life, our vaunted portion ?
Unfulfilment, doubt, despair !
Why waste all our days in caution
Lest we miss our little share ?
Will, that lacks performing power,
Strength, we know not how to use ;
This is all our glorious dower,
This, the soul we would not lose !
Ah, we feel our impulse leaping
At the suffering and the wrong,
At the moaning and the weeping
Of the weak beneath the strong.
Reel a strength to strike and vanquish
Rising passionate within,
Strength to cure the bitter anguish,
Triumph o’er the arts of sin.
Why do we for ever falter,
Ever fear to strike our best ?
Why with our emotions palter
Till they sink in deadened rest?
Eor, in vain an outlet seeking,
Soon the spark has lost its heat,
While the sorrow still lies shrieking
Round our long-delaying feet?
Is it best to keep the middle
Path, that gives us comfort cold ?
Care no more to loose the riddle,
Take the gilt, and call it gold ?
Ever doomed to failure dreary
Oftentimes we fall and cry ;
And our souls, of labor weary,
Pray for nothing but to die.
All is empty, useless, hollow—
Eor we know our hopeless clay ;
For we know we dare not follow
Where we point the upward way.
Honor, nobleness, perfection—
Are but words we learn by rote;
Were they spoken with reflection,
They would choke the lying throat.
Through the shrouds that close our vision
Comes no ray of any star,
Comes no voice to tell our mission,
Whence we come, and what we are.
All our poetry and story
Cannot light the vaulted gloom ;
Even Christ’s unmeasured glory
Shines above an empty tomb.
Yio.
WYVIS.
THE EARTH IS THE LORD’S.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On the front of the Royal Exchange, London, is “The
earth is the Lord’s, and the fullness, thereof.”
‘‘The earth is the Lord’s, and the fullness thereof
They have carved it in letters of stone
On the mart where the modern pirate stand,
Plotting to seize in his soft, white hands
The harvest the poor have sown.
“The earth is the Lord’s, and the fullness thereof,”
And the poor are the Lord’s great care ;
But His priests’ great care is the pirate’s soul.
For their prayers are bought with the heavy toll
That is wrung from the toilers’ share.
“The earth is the Lord’s, and the fullness thereof
Yet His people that delve therein <
Are the muzzled oxen, faint with blows,
Who, starving, tread out the corn for those
That neither toil nor spin.
“The earth is the Lord’s and the fullness thereof,”
And gold shares shall spring from the sword ;
So the bishop blesses the Maxim gun,
For his shares will rise when its work is done,
And the blacks are reaped for the Lord.
The earth is John Bull’s and the fullness thereof;
And wherever his brood may range
They carry his message with shot and shell—
The black man’s land for the white man’s hell 1
For that is a Royal Exchange.
CRIPPS CLARK.
EXISTENCE: A DEFINITION.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
’Tis a strange disease where we laugh and weep
And are lulled at times in the arms of sleep ;
’Tis a passing flash, ’tis a waking dream,
’Tis a wave that lifts on an endless stream ;
’Tis a mystic trance where the things we see
Are the shadows faint of the things that be ;
’Tis a goblet that’s mingled of joy and of fear,
Tis a pendulum swung ’twixt a smile and a tear;
Tis a battle for bread and for love and for breath,
’Tis a race for life to the jaws of death 1

IMPORTANT NOTICE TO CIGARETTE SMOKERS.
WE beg to inform our customers and cigarette smokers generally that the American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., of N.S.W., who control the sale of cigarettes known under the brands of “Allen and
Ginter’s Straight-cut No. 1,” “Cameo ” and “ Old Judge,” and who manufacture the colonial-made
cigarettes called “ Pure Gold ” and “ Dixon’s No. 1,” have given us official notice that they will not
supply us with any of their American-made cigarettes unless we agree to relinquish the agency held by
us for New South Wales for the world-renowned English firm of W. D. and H. O. WILLS, Limited,
the manufacturers of those popular high-class cigarettes known as
“tHZS.EE CASTLES” and “ CAPSTAU.”
We have declined to conform with this request, as we feel it would not be to the interest of
cigarette smokers in New South Wales to allow the American Tobacco Co.. Limited, to monopolise
the whole of the cigarette trade. We, therefore, appeal to cigarette smokers to try the celebrated
Wills’ “Three Castles” and “Capstan” Cigarettes,
And compare their quality with that of any other. We are confident that their verdict will be in
favor of the
“ Three Castles” and ‘‘Capstan” Cigarettes,
And by smoking these they will prevent any one Company from attempting to dictate what people-
should or should not smoke.
HEYDE, TODMAN & CO. (Aaents for W. D. and H. 0. WILLS, Ltd.)

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin- Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order , One Pound
Note, or stamps of any Australian produce, you can
have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week for
a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney . In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our earners, with whom we do not wish to
interfere.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, Managing Director.
Literary Communications to be addressed J. F,
Archibald.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Sho7-t Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly co7ifidential.
The Bulletin will return all unavailable MS. when
the address is specified arid stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
ice are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To Amatew's in Black and White,
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event, will
be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture containing
a good or adaptable idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion—i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions wider
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS,
Copies of The Bulletin can be obtained in London,
after the arrival of each mail, at the office of the British
Australasian Co., Limited, SI Fleet Street, E.C.
Also in the Transvaal -.—The Standard and Diggers
News Co., Limited, Johannesburg, South Africa.
Also:— R. A. Thompson and Co., 3 Church Street,
Capetown, South Africa.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 10, 1895.
Queensland Finance.
The great problem of Constitutional Govern-
ment in Britain is to find materials for one
efficient Ministry out of nearly 40,000,000
people, and the exceedingly bad results
which are occasionally arrived at show that,
even out of 40,000,000 people, it is often
difficult to find half-a-dozen really able states-
men. The problem in Australasia is how to
find materials for seven efficient Ministries
out of 4,000,000 people, which, on an arith-
metical basis, is just about 70 times as
difficult a question. Therefore, as a natural
consequence, Ministries in this country are
mostly feeble and inefficient, and not any
more honest than might reasonably be ex-
pected. And as the Treasurer’s position is
the one which presents most difficulties and
demands most intelligence, and as even
Great Britain is only able to find, out of her
40,000,000 people, a commanding financial
genius once in a long while or thereabouts,
and as Australia is still less able to find seven
commanding financial geniuses out of one-
tenth the population, it comes to pass
that the average Treasurer in this country
is generally a great and dismal failure.
Under these circumstances, the fact that
Hugh Nelson is Premier and Treasurer of
Queensland is, perhaps, a shade less astonish-
ing than it seems, though the presence of
Nelson at the head of a Government is an
astonishing fact under any circumstances.
• • •
Queensland, for a number of years past,
has possessed the most crooked Government,
and the most bankrupt Treasury, and the
most dishonest financial policy in Australia.
Under the party system the business of
every Treasurer, like that of every other
Minister, is to do his best, not for the
country but for his party —to spend money
lavishly in order to retain his supporters ;
to keep the taxes down to the lowest possible
point; and, with all this, to make the finances
look as if they were straight, whether they
are or not. Therejs absolutely no reason,
so far as the party is concerned, why they
should be straight ; it is only necessary that
they should seem as if they were. Conse-
quently, almost every Treasurer is politically
dishonest, partly because almost everybody is
dishonest, and partly by I’eaSon of his special
inducements to dishonesty. The Queensland
financiers of late year’s, however, have had
more inducements to- gp crooked than the
majority of their species. The political
party now in power practically owns the
Queensland National Bank, and the Q.N.
Bank owns it, more or less, in return ; in
fact, the personnel of the Government party
and that of the bank are so largely identical
that it is often difficult to tell them apart.
The Q.N. Bank is supported by the Govern-
ment to such a liberal extent that not very
much less than one-half its deposits are State
money —much of it, seemingly, borrowed for
the bank’s purposes ; and it is sufficiently
obvious that the sudden withdrawal of these
deposits, at this distressful period, would
shake that institution very violently indeed.
If the Government goes out, that event
implies the advent to power of a party which
has no direct interest in supporting the Q. N.
Bank, and which might do something to it
which would bring woeful disaster on its
shareholder's—who are largely the party now
supporting the Nelson Ministry. Therefore
the ruling crowd of the Assembly has more
reason to keep itself in power by any process,
no matter how desperate, than has any
other Ministerial following in Australasia.
Whether any of its doings actually arise from
this motive is a mere matter of conjecture,
but the motive, at all events, exists.
« » •
This state of things does not conduce to
good Government, and the Ministerial
policy in Queensland, from whatever
cause, has been, for many years past,
even more reckless than that of Victoria
at the boom-time. Queensland has now
a debt of £74 per head, nearly twice
that of Victoria, and the heaviest debt, in
proportion to population, on this earth.
Much of it was incurred in order to build
railways across empty wastes, in order that
the land in the vicinity might be rendered
saleable, so that the proceeds might be
dumped into the Treasury, to save it from
bankruptcy. Queensland has now more rail-
way, in proportion to population, than any
other country in the world, Westralia alone
excepted. The interest on its public debt is
£2 18s. Id. per annum for each inhabitant—
the biggest interest bill in all this globe of
debt and bankruptcy. It has habitually
raised a larger proportion of its revenue by
land-sales than any other province, New
South Wales alone excepted ; and it has
had, for many years, the largest average
expenditure per head, again with the excep-
tion of New South Wales. Also, it is the
only province of Australasia where the
colored labor question has grown to dangerous
dimensions, the only province where the
working-classes are kept down in the Fat-
man interest by a Coercion Act, and
where employers and employes are periodi-
cally in a state that borders on civil
war, and where two-thirds of the pro-
vince is retained against its will by the
Parliamentary majority of the other third.
Queensland, with its debts and difficul-
ties, its Kanaka and Chinese question,
its Separation Question, its annual dis-
turbances in the West, its suppression
of the Opposition voice in the Legislature
by the brute violence -of the Government
majority, its truly remarkable bank which
owns the Government party and is also
owned by it, and its equally remarkable
judicial system which also seems to be owned
by the Government majority, has almost
given up professing to be a self-governing
country run on Parliamentary principles.
* # *
Queensland, as Treasurer Nelson candidly
remarked in his recent financial statement,
has not had a surplus for many years past.
Also, it has not had an honest surplus, or
one which could bear investigation, for many
years longer. Queensland’s surpluses were
made, in the first place, by charging
ordinary current expenditure to loan-
moneys, and thus £10,000,000 has been
spent in various ways which yield no
revenue whatever —sunk in the bottom-
less gulf of wild-cat finance. A lot
more were produced by the simple
process of selling land at almost any price
to whoever would buy. M‘llwraith con-
stantly squared his finances in this fashion ;
he even conceived the stupendous idea of
selling practically all the remaining public
lands of the province in one lot to a British
railway syndicate, but the scheme somehow
fell through. But at last a time came when
land sales declined, not for want of land, but
because every bank and syndicate and
private speculator in Queensland was gorged
with this kind of property and required time
to unload. Then Queensland began to
accumulate a series of deficits which speedily
mounted up to something over £1,400,000.
These were staved off for the time being by
the issue of Treasury bills, and it was
decided to liquidate the bills by a new series
of land sales, the price being reduced to 10s.
per acre in order to induce local and foreign
capitalists to make a special effort and
swallow still more territory. But at this price
the special land sales only realised between
£400,000 and £500,000. Then the M‘ll-
wraijh - Griffith - Nelson Ministry made
the greatest financial effort of its life. This
special fund was authorised solely because
there was a huge deficiency to be liquidated,
yet not one penny of it went for that pur-
pose. It was all absorbed into current
revenue, so that the Ministry actually made
over £400,000 of new revenue out of its pre-
vious shortages. Then it floated a new loan
with a 60 years’ currency, for the purpose of
paying off the aforesaid Treasury bills,, and
£1,128,000 of the deficit is thus being
passed on to posterity as usual. ’ The balance
will doubtless be similarly dealt with later
on. The loan, by the way, was floated fully
’nine months before it was wanted, and the
province is paying interest on it in the mean-
time to the extent of over £31,000, which
represents another large sum gone into the
abyss of Kanakaland finance.
* # *
In addition to this £1,400,000 shortage
which is in process of being passed on to
posterity, Queensland has another floating
deficiency of £296,000 or thereabouts, which
further complicates the situation. Treasurer
Nelson announced the other day that the
province had, for the first time in many
years, a surplus of £104,000, and that the
floating deficiency was thus reduced to
£192,000. He also announced that the sur-
plus was to be formed into the nucleus
of a sinking-fund to pay off the public
debt, or at least the Treasury bills already
mentioned. It likewise appears from his
somewhat confused statements that there
are outstanding liabilities of the past year to
the amount of, perhaps, £150,000. There-
fore, Mr. • Nelson’s surplus apparently
stands somewhat as follows: —(1) There
is no real surplus, the £104,0b0 being
not nearly sufficient to cover the out-
standing liabilities of the year ; (2) If there
was a surplus, it could not be appropriated
to a sinking-fund according to any sane
financial process, as the Treasury has a
floating deficiency of £296,000 to meet ; (3)
If there was a surplus, which there isn’t, the
Treasury also owes £400,000 which it took
out of the special land sales account, and
which it is morally, thdugh not legally,
bound to repay ; (4) Treasurer Nelson pro-
fesses, by means of his surplus, to have
reduced the floating debt from £296,000 to
about £192,000. He is also going to vest
the surplus in the hands of “ independent
trustees ” as the nucleus of a sinking-fund,
and as the alleged amount, even if it existed,
can’t be in two places at once, it is obviously
impossible that he can do both these things
with it. To put the matter shortly, the sur-
plus of £104,000, if there is one, has been
arrived at by borrowing £1,128,000 and
putting it into revenue ; it has also been
arrived at in part by continuing to annex as
revenue the proceeds of special land-sales
which were originally instituted for a very
different purpose, and the rest by borrowing
a large sum from a wholly unaccountable
“trust account”; and, despite these two
different ways of bringing it about there is
no reliable surplus, and it is all, and more
than all, owing if there was one, and Trea-
surer Nelson’s proposal to do two separate
and distinct things with the money which is
already owing in four different places is an
absurdity in any case.
• * •
The ghastly humor of Nelson’s finance is
that it professes to represent the beginning
of a new and brighter era. The Treasurer
acknowledges that his predecessors have not
done all that they should have done in the
past, but he points with pride to the fact
that in future the financial administration of
the country is to be honest and economical
and capable of bearing the closest inspection.
The glaring and childishly clumsy process by
which his budget is juggled into an appear-
ance of partial solvency is interesting, as the
beginning of this financial reformation. What
Nelson’s finance would be like if he hajtnlt
reformed, and was merely going on in the
old, bad way, passes all speculation. The
only thing that can be said in his favor is
that his devices fire not quite so foolish, or
his figures so ridiculous, or his present
deficit quite so vast, as those of his con-
temporary, G. H. Reid, of New South
Wales. And that is but feeble commenda-
tion at the best.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
Even-handed Justice.
Whether or no the Rev. Jas. Clarke has
been shabbily diddled out of his legal rights is,
of course, a question about which nobody very
much cares. The Rev. Jas. Clarke is the
Sydney cleric who objected to his wife wearing
stays on the ground that the Mother of the
Saviour never wore stays; and he Js also
alleged to have rolled his lady, in a nude state,
round a considerable extent of paddock strewn
with pebbles and jam-tins, in order that her
flesh might be subjected to his spirit in a suffi-
ciently orthodox degree. There is no doubt
that Mr. Clarke is most eccentric, and little
that Mrs. C. and her mamma knew something
of his eccentricity before the marriage,
but—if you want to be a vicar’s wife you
must make allowances. Anyway, Mrs. Clarke
had no difficulty in convincing Judge Win-
deyer that she had been grossly ill-used,
and a decree nisi in divorce was granted
in February, and made absolute on May 27.
Previous to this, and after his wife left
him, Mr. Clarke had been once confined
in a mad - house and once in a lunacy
reception-house—without preliminary medical
examination, and simply at the whim of the
police. Qualified doctors found nothing in his
condition to justify detention, and he was
released. His second arrest was a quite un-
necessary outrage, and Mr. Clarke asserts
(no doubt incorrectly) that it only oc-
curred because he quoted Luke xviii.,
6, after the divorce verdict had been
given—“ Hear what the unjust judge saith !”
When Mr. Clarke came out of the reception-
house, he proceeded to appeal to the Full
Court against Judge Windeyer’s rule abso-
lute—as was his undoubted right. His legal
position was this : if able to give security
for costs he had to ascertain what security
would be satisfactory to the Judge or Registrar
of the Supreme Court, and provide it within 21
days. If unable to give security for costs, ho
could sue as a pauper. It was his duty to
make affidavit that he was a pauper, and to
\pply to the Registrar for a certificate to that
effect within 21 days. Mr. Clarke, as an
undoubted pauper, complied with the latter con-
ditions. He applied to the Registrar for a certi-
ficate, which it is apparently quite within that
officer’s power to grant if he chooses, as soon as
he is satisfied of the truth of the affidavit.* Tho
Registrar does not appear to have questioned
the truth of the affidavit, yet he did not grant
the certificate, but told Mr. Clarke to go to
a Judge.. By this time Mr. Clarke must
have realised what an objectionable litigant he
was—if he did not realise it before. He went
to the Chief Justice, who has undoubtedly
power to order the Registrar to grant the cer-
tificate, and the Chief Justice told him to go to
the Full Court. He went to the Full. Court
and found sitting there Judge Windeyer, who
told him to come again when the.. Full Court
was differently constituted, Air this time,
of course, the 21 days of grace were grow-
ing beautifully less ; and before the Full
Court was differently constituted they ex-
pired altogether. And still the wretched
parson, tossed from pillar to post, had not got
his official certificate of poverty—neither, of
course, had he given security for costs. Be it
remembered that Mr. Clarke was not yet
appealing : he was only applying for per-
mission to appeal permission to use his
legal right to be heard in his own cause.
The Registrar so far listened to his plea for
justice that he set the appeal down for heal ing ;
and on July 31 this burlesque of justice was
enacted : Mr. Clarke, too poor to employ a
solicitor, appears in person, and details the cir-
cumstances recited above. Mrs. Clarke’s
counsel objects. No security has been given
for costs ; there is no certificate of poverty ;
the appeal ought never to have been set down.
Mr. Clarke pathetically explains. Judge
Owen says that he should have applied for his
certificate of poverty. Mr. Clarke explains
some more. Judge Simpson asks him can he
not give security for costs. Mr. Clarke con-
tinues to explain. The Chief Justice sums up.
He says Mr. Clarke has not given security for
costs, and he has not presented his certificate
of poverty. Therefore, Mr. Clarke is out of
court. He can’t sue as a pauper now, because
he has no certificate ; and he can’t apply for his
certificate, because the 21 days of grace have
lapsed. Therefore there is actually no appeal
before the court. Therefore the appeal is dis-
missed with costs. And if Mr. Clarre doesn’t
pay the costs of this appeal which never
existed, he can presumably be sent to gaol in
the ordinary course. The Bulletin is proud
of the N.S.W. Supreme Court.

One Law for the Rich, Another for the

Melbourne’s latest legal scandal— t.e., latest
at time of writing—was the case of one
Roberts, a solicitor, charged before Justice
a’Beckett with the particular form of mis-
appropriation which, when practised by mem-

“ unbecoming conduct.” A succinct account
of this gentleman’s little indiscretion may be
quoted from the learned Judge’s summing-up :
With reference to the charges brought against Mr.
Roberts, lie would deal with that which was the more
serious first, and that was a charge perfectly plain and
free from any sort of complication as to what he might be
accused of doing. It was that being a co-trustee with two
other men, and acting as solicitor for the trust, he was
given £46!) for investment and had not invested it. In
answer to enquiries by one of the trustees he said: “It is
all right. It is in good security.” But, eventually,
when no further excuses were available, he con-
fessed that it had not been invested. Then, under
pressure, he gave them security for the money. It
was also clear -that the money ha ded to him for
investment was p]dd into his own account, that he

Justice A’Beckett, it will be observed, couldn’t
get away from the fact that solicitor Roberts
had converted a client’s trust money to his own
uses, and told the thinnest of lies in defence of
his dishonesty, but because the man (was a
solicitor, and not an unprotected variety of
trustee?) his Honor seemed to think it necessary
to drop the truth gently. It is not thus that
Judges (outside of Melbourne) speak of ordin-
ary people who gamble with 'Coin entrusted to
them for safe keeping. Justice a’Beckett,
having gingerly suggested time Roberts’ ‘‘ ex-
planation ” was so “bald and insufficient” as
to be obvious nonsense, proceeded to shed a
beautiful soft legal light on the situation,
“There was the view that Mr. Roberts
deliberately left the money to take its chance.
He was engaged in large transactions” [so is
the clerk who plunges on the Cup race with
money * borrowed ’ from his confiding em-
ployer], “ and as he was a wealthy man
he probably thought no harm would come
of it through dealing with it in this manner.”
Justice a’Beckett found some excuse for the
gentleman’s crooked dealing in his conscious-
ness of wealth , although the land-boom exploded
before he took possession of the <£469, and his
schedule was filed before he admitted that the
money had gone into his private account. The
co-trustees have been compelled to make good
the .£469 to the estate. It would puzzle the
learned Judge to find an excuse for the continu-
ation of the gentleman’s crooked dealing after
bankruptcy had begun to stare him in the face,
or to prove that Roberts wasn’t fairly started
on the road to bankruptcy when he first
“ left the money to take its chance.”
That was in May, 1889. Everybody’s dream
of wealth was already vanishing at that time.
But no matter. Justice a’Beckett gave the
dishonest solicitor the benefit of the wealth
theory, and then found himself “ relieved of
the obligations of taking the more serious view
which might be taken, yet considered that Mr.
Roberts should not go unpunished.” So, Mr.
Roberts was suspended from the practice of
his honorable profession —0, ye gods ! —for one
year. In twelve calendar months he will be a
protected solicitor once again. The joke of
this sentence upon Roberts lies chiefly in the
preamble to his Honor’s judgment. “In the
interests of the public and the reputation of
the profession, the view with which the court
regarded lawyers’ delinquencies had to be con-
sidered. It would be a lamentable thing if any
idea of indifference to these interests were to
prevail with the public, or that it should be
supposed that the court acted on a low view of
the moral and legal obligations of legal prac-
titioners.” It is lamentable that the aforesaid
idea does prevail, and that Judge a’Beckett
has helped to strengthen it.
The Three Laws.
There is one law for the rich, another for the
poor, and a third for people connected with
the legal profession. That case of the Mel-
bourne solicitor’s clerk, a junior, whose em-
bezzlements earned only a modest fine from
magistrate Panton, was the “curtain-raiser”
preceding the piece de resistance. Next day,
one Gillies pleaded guilty in Melbourne Crimi-
nal Court to “larceny as a servant” of the
same legal firm which figured in the police-
court farce. Gillies was the confidential or
managing clerk, and had worked a system of
embezzlement in conjunction with the junior
culprit. There were no extenuating features
in the dishonesty of Gillies. He is the
typical flash bachelor of 34, the man about
town who goes astray with a big cigar between
his teeth, plays cards at the club, and fools
around methodically. He was arrested on a
warrant in W.A., and had a “ real good time”
on his way back to Melbourne —as good a time
as circumstances would permit. When he
sailed, in care of the detective, from Adelaide, a
number of rollicking sympathisers gave him a
“ send off,” and, having arrived at Melbourne,
he proceeded to “ do ” the Block, and his other
familiar haunts, on bail. If he showed any signs
of penitence or shame for his discreditable
position, they were not visible through the
smoke of his bulky havannah. Perhaps there was
ho occasion for penitence, seeing that fraudulent
lawyers are indigenous to Melb. ,and Gillieswrs
merely in the fashion. Nevertheless, his jaunty
demeanor prior to coming up for sentence
deserves special mention, as indicating a legal
sense of security under conditions which might
have given a commercial traveller, or a
draper’s assistant, much anxiety. Gillies, as
already stated, was a systematic embezzler, and
responsible for his confederate’s crime. His
“larcenies” were many and frequent. He
pleaded guilty to two specified loots. Justice
Hood then said that the case was very “diffi-
cult to deal with ’’—being “ that of a man who
held a respectable position and had plunged
himself into crime.” What Justice Hood might
have said was that the “difficulty” in the
way of dealing fairly with Gillies was
the prisoner’s line of business. . His particular
respectability lay in his connection with the
almighty law. Justice Hood, it seems, felt so
concerned about his duty to Gillies that he
consulted another Judge, and the upshot of
their deliberations was the immediate release
of the unfortunate “bounder” on his own re-
cognisances, and one surety of .£5O. The free
and frequent embezzler was accorded “a second
chance,” chiefly on the ground that “ his em-
ployers appeared to have been quite content at
one time that he should have escaped without
further punishment” than the loss of his billet.
This cheerful encouragement to the robbery of
kindly-disposed employers, coming from a
Judge, would sound strange but for the fact
that Gillies belonged to the privileged pro-
fession. One law for the rich, another for the
poor, and a third for the lawyers.
Fair Dinkum.
The Nemesis that seldom overtakes commercial
crime has seen fit to wreak mild vengeance
upon the importing firm of Lazarus, Rosen-
fkld and Benjamin. Four men concerned in
the management of the firm’s Melbourne busi-
ness were last week sentenced to imprisonment
and fines for “conspiring to defraud Her
Majesty’s Customs.” Tins description of the
offence is misleading. It was not a case of
merely “ chousing ” the Victorian revenue out
of a certain amount of duty chargeable upon cer-
tain goods; it was a case of stealing cash from
the exchequer by means of excessive drawback
claims. Lazarus, Rosenfeld and Co., having
paid duty to the Victorian Customs depart-
ment on account of foreign fancy goods
landed at Melbourne, transhipped the stuff
to Sydney or other Australian ports at
an advanced valuation, and received from
the department more “ drawback ” money than
they had paid. The difference between the
original duty and subsequent drawback repre-
sented theft to that amount. Messrs. Rosen-
feld, Davis, Benjamin and Stanway have
properly gone to gaol, and three of them are
working out their terms with hard labor. Their
case exhibits the immorality of Melbourne
sentiment in a strong light. When the men
received their sentences they were said to have
“ hard luck ! ” in a comparative way of speak-
ing. It was “ hard luck !•” because other con-
victed Customs swindlers have escaped im-
prisonment, and the great mass of such
swindlers have hitherto escaped prosecution.
The common acceptance of this “ hard luck ! ”
theory recalls Alfred Deakin’s barristerial
plea for the financial rogue—“ they all
did it in boom time.” In Melbourne’s
mercantile world they have all done it
always, and with the advance in Customs
duties they have done it more than ever.
“ Lines ” shipped under an undutiable (and
generally correct) heading, have been re-shipped
as dutiable, every penny of drawback in those
cases being stolen from the State. The secret
history of the softgoods warehouses stinks of
criminal offence; intercolonial “trade” in
many things is only a bogus dealing between
two branches of the same business for the pur-
pose of drawback robbery. Under-statements
of cost are almost as much the rule as the excep-
tion. The dishonest trader denounced by Jus-
tice Hodges, who “ dresses in purple and fine
linen, and rides in a sumptuously-cushioned
carriage, whilst the honest trader is driven to
insolvency or the streets,” is a übiquitous
Barabbas. Everybody seems to be seized, as
they say in the senates, of his malefactions,
and most people complacently regard them as
the natural outcome of Victorian government,
the product of political corruption. If banking
and land-company swindles have been per-
petrated with impunity, why should the dis-
honored arm of the law strike at the black
heart of commerce, and, if it must strike, why
not hit fair ? What have Rosenfeld and his
colleagues done that is worse than the doings
of a hundred other silk-hatted tricksters ?
There is some comfort in the shocking indig-
nation with which Collins-st. mutters this
enquiry. Nobody demanded positive justice
for the community during the years when
fraud ran riot, but now that the “potting”
of the miserable Abrahams crowd has
been followed by the “jugging” of the
Rosenfeld party, a desire for comparative jus-
tice fills the air, and tends to make it sweeter.
Every sinful calico-jimmy roaming at large is
an insult to the doctrine of “fair dinkum”
which all men, rogues or otherwise, hold in
respect. Let Nemesis take her course since
she has been permitted to taste a little Hebrew
blood. Give the Presbyterians, Wesleyans,
Baptists, and Episcopal Church supporters a
chance to say their prayers in a dungeon-cell !
Not for the sake of the national character and
public honour, but in the name of “ fair
dinkum.”
Fat Man Patriotism.
The Fat Men of Sydney Chamber of Commerce
had an unusual outbreak of candor the other
day, when the committee presented its annual
report with the following clause embodied
therein:
The community have watched with great interest the
recent taxation proposals of the Government; but, while
the major portion are undoubtedly Freetraders, they are
somewhat inclined to modify their views when they find
at what cost they are to obtain Freetrade.
This evidently meant that the committee-men,
being importers, were also Freetraders, so long
as some one else had to pay for Freetrade, but
when the price of that policy was exacted from
themselves in the shape of a land and income
tax, they began to think they would just as
soon have the other thing. They were not sure
about the point, but were in a state of sus-
pended fiscal animation, waiting to see what
the price would amount to. The fact that they
professed to speak of “the community” rather
than of themselves didn’t really alter the brutal
candor of the statement ; the obvious fact
remained that the committee had no defined
views apart from its own interests, and had
merely pretended to have Freetrade convic-
tions, and that it only professed to think that
its professed Freetrade convictions were bene-
ficial to the country, because they were profit-
able to itself. The clause was almost allowed
to pass unchallenged, but at the last
moment Mr. S. A. Joseph drew atten-
tion to it, and then the Chamber struck it
out —no doubt feeling that it was advertising its
want of political principle too glaringly. Never-
theless, if S. A. Joseph hadn’t moved in the
matter, the signs are that the rest of the Cham-
ber would have let the statement pass without
dissent. The average Fat Man feels that way ;
the only trouble is that a certain amount of
nerve is required to stand up in front of an
aggressive person named Joseph, and maintain
that he feels that way for purely patriotic and
unselfish reasons.
A Point of Law.
The New South Wales courts have been wrest-
ling at great length with an abstruse question
about the delivery of a summons. The law
-tates that, in certain cjvses, “ notice in writing
-hall be delivered ” to defendant or “ left at his
usual place of abode.” In the present instance
the notice was delivered to him in an envelope
by the postman, and it was also left at his usual
place of abode, he being there when the post-
man arrived. There are no stipulations in the
Act as to whether it is to be left or delivered
by a postman or by any other specified indivi-
dual ; the only legal requirement is that the
document is to get there. I he defendant,
however, claimed that this was not delivery
“ within the meaning of the Act,” and Judge
Fxtzhardingk nonsuited plaintiff accordingly.
There was an appeal to the Supreme Court,
and two expensive Judges, three barristers and
four solicitors wrestled with the question.
Finally they came to the decision that as the
Act said the document was to be delivered, and
nothing more, therefore it simply meant that it
was to be delivered, and whether it was delivered
by a postman, or a vagrant, or a red-headed
woman, or a person with bow-legs didn’t matter
so long as it was delivered, and as defendant
admitted that it was delivered, and had ad-
mitted it all along, therefore the Court’s
decision was that it had been delivered.
Accordingly, the case was sent back to be tried,
the two separate bills of costs incurred up to
date not having advanced the real question at
issue by so much as one inch. The fact that it
took one Judge, two barristers, two solicitors,
and one bill of costs to decide in the first place
that the paper hadn’t been delivered, though
the defendant admitted getting it, and two
Judges, seven barristers and solicitors, and
another bill of costs to decide afterwards that,
as the defendant got it, it must have actually
arrived, is a beautiful evidence of the learned
profundity of the law. Evidently what New
South Wales wants now is a statute setting
forth that when a document has arrived at its
destination that fact shall be sufficient evidence
that it has got there, and that if it has really
got there in the ordinary sense of the term it
shall also have done it “ within the meaning of
the Act.”
The Absentee.
When the N.S.W. Land and Income Tax Bill
makes its next appearance before Parliament,
two or three anomalous features, which passed
unnoticed on its first introduction, might be
amended with great advantage. One relates to
the situation of the foreign mortgagee. The
bill places the local mortgagee and the local
landowner on the same footing, rightly con-
sidering that the former is, to all intents and
purposes, a landowner for the time being. But
though it lays a special tax—much too small a
one, however —on the chronically-absent land-
holder, it lays no extra impost whatever on the
absent mortgagee. If the latter forecloses his
mortgage, and becomes the nominal owner of
the land, then the extra tax falls upon him
straightway, but until then the fact that he is
an absentee is wholly disregarded. According
to all reason and logic the absent mortgagee
and the absent landholder should stand on the
same footing. Again, though the private
absentee landholder is specially discouraged,
though in a pitiably ineffective fashion, the
absentee corporation is, so far as can be gathered
from the text of the measure, wholly disre-
garded. The landholding company which has
its head office, chief managers, and all, or the
majority of, its shareholders in England, and
which sends the great bulk of its profits to
that country, is as distinctly an absentee as
any private individual can be, and the
fact that it may have a branch office and a
deputy manager, and a subordinate board of
directors, and, possibly, a few casual share-
holders in Australia, makes no real difference.
Moreover, it is much more in need of active
discouragement than the private owner, partly
because it represents a very much larger evil,
and partly because it is the more incurable
absentee of the two. The bill may possibly
be intended to cover absentee corporations, but
in that case it is much too vague and ineffectual
to serve its purpose. It states that any
“ owner ” who has not resided in New South
Wales for four out of the preceding six years
shall be liable to pay an extra tax ; and the in-
terpretation clause explains that an owner
means any person who possesses land, and,
further on, that the word “person” includes
“ company.” But as it altogether fails to state
that the possession of a local office for the pur-
pose of collecting the loot and despatching it to
England does not constitute residence, the
foreign landholding corporation stands the
best possible chance of escaping the extra tax,
along with his brother and accomplice, the
foreign mortgagee. And a third important
discrepancy is that there is no provision for
levying a special income-tax on the absentee,
as well as a special land-tax. Therefore
the incomes derived by residents abroad from
gold and other mines, house-rents, annuities,
or from any other source save land, the
large sums earned in the province by
foreign banks, and life, fire, and marine insur-
ance companies, the profits from money invested
in liens in wool and stock, and from miscellaneous
trading enterprises, all go free of any special
tax. And when these are added to the vast
amounts taken out of the country by foreign
mortgagees and landholding companies, there is
so little absentee-tax left as not to be worth
the name. Moreover, in one astounding
instance the absentee is specially released from
all tax while the local resident is not. Among
the income-tax exemptions is < “ Income accru-
ing to any person not resident in Neiv South
Wales from Government debentures.” If the
exemption covered all debentures sold prior to
the passing of the Act it would be understand-
able, as to sell debentures at a fixed rate of
interest and then deduct a tax would be some-
thing very like a breach of faith. But Premier
Reid proposes to deliberately perpetrate this
breach of faith on the local investor, while the
foreigner goes free, now and for ever after-
wards. In other words, the foreign money-
lender is offered a higher rate of interest than
the local one, and a special effort is made to
prevent New South Wales ever becoming inde-
pendent of the British Shyi.ock. This is about
the worst inversion of the absentee-tax idea on
record.

Barrister Duffy’s gibe, in the Abrahams
case, that those lovely rascals were being pro-
secntecl nob merely because they were gun-
forgers, but because they were Jews, would
appear in the light of later events to have con
siderable foundation in fact. While the cata.
lepbic ones are scarcely entered on the era ol
their 1,2 months hard, another Hebrew firm i.i
criminally arraigned for falsifying manifest*
and swindling the Customs out of “divers
moneys.” Ib is duly found guilty ; the pro-
prietor gets nine months, t lie manager and
book-keeper six months, and the shipping-clerk
(he, no doubt, had to help the swindle or lose
his billet) three months—the principals also
having to pay commensurate fines. So far,
so good, but the Jewish faction down
South is pertinently asking why a certain
saintly Christian jeweller, who has systemati-
cally diddled the Customs on week-days and
piously turned up at church on Sundays, should
have been allowed to go free of prosecution on
paying a huge sum to the revenue ; why another
bowled-out Kerristian jeweller satisfied the
authorities by dropping his little mite in the
slot; and why the genial head of a most respect-
able Gentile firm, who, it was lately found, had
buncoed the revenue of his confiding country
for many months, is not now doing lapidary
work in Pentridge Stockade beside Abrahams
and Rosenfeld. The Chosen race seem to be
chosen with a vengeance.
Ah accused person awaiting trial is supposed to
be treated as innocent until he is found guilty ;
but in N.S. Wales, if unable to get bail, he is
not only held in confinement, but also subjected
to penal discipline. Prisoners committed for
trial are not allowed to see newspapers or
receive any general information from the out-
side world. They are compelled to attend a
church service twice a week, or be confined
in cells while the service is going on.
They are allowed to smoke only dark
twist tobacco, and must use a clay pipe. •
Should a warder behave improperly by, say,
striking a man awaiting trial, and the victim,
in complaining, apply any derogatory epithet
to the bully, he is locked up in a separ-
ate cell till the visiting magistrate sits,
and tried for a prison crime. All this is under-
stood to be warranted by the regulations made
by the Governor-in-Council. Now, a prisoner
who is not yet tried is a citizen who is giving
the security of his body for his appearance
before the court, and not a person who has
become liable to anything different from the
ordinary laws of the land. Such a man should
not be subjected to treatment to which an out-
side citizen is not subject, except in so far as
requisite to hold his person for trial. But,
according to the practice, any man —at all
events, any poor man—can be taken out of the
operation of the ordinary laws of the land, and
subjected to a different set of laws of the most
arbitrary and tyrannical kind, at the simple
pleasure of anyone who will cause him to be
arrested, and of any magistrate who will fix a
prohibitory bail.

A London paper affects to be shocked by the
bad taste of certain music-hall verses which
assume the guilt of Jabez Balfour —or Mr.
Spencer Balfour, as the writer styles him.
Fat Man’s cry of “Shame ! ” upon people who
roughly and readily criticised the Melbourne
bank-busters a while ago is recalled to memory
by this solicitude for poor dear “ Mr.
Spencer.” The fact is that Balfour has stood
convicted of crooked dealing ever since the
figures of his hopelessly insolvent financial
institutions were published. Also, he pleaded
guilty by his flight to Argentina, and his
persistent refusal to leave that place of refuge.
Whether he gets sent to prison or nob has
nothing bo do with the case as judged by
the public to whom popular rhymesters appeal.
The bloated creature richly deserves punish-
ment, but he would never have been put in
the way to receive it if the unjudicially-
minded masses hadn’t “ stuck to their man,”
so to speak, and yelled for the police until the
Rosebery Government gave the signal for his
arrest, an event that took a long time to trans-
pire, anyhow. Should Balfour’s financial
transactions escape a fair trial, and an acquittal
of the prisoner be accomplished by “ influence ”
for influential reasons, the accusing figures will
still stand in the books, and if in the general
British opinion they look as black as ever, the
general opinion will remain undisturbed. Also,
ib will express itself in a more or less riotous
manner. Victoria seems bo be the only English-
speaking country where hard facts are easily
dissolved in bucketfuls of legal drivel, and
nobody objects to the vanishing-trick.
S.A, Co., the bloated absentee concern
which has sweated rents out of S.A. producers for
nearly 60 years, lately held its annual meeting in
London, and much bad language was expended
over the “ decreased div. through progressive land
taxation in the colony.” This is something for
the Tory papers to digest. When progressive
taxation was proposed it was alleged that the poor
producer would have to pay. But he doesn’t.
He can’t pay any more and live, so the London
man must give up a little of his turkey and fizz.
Staughton, M.L.A., a squat squatter in
Vic. Assembly, objected to the Govt, proposals re
the duty on monumental stones, the other night,
because, “under the proposal, he believed the
poor man would have to pay more for his tomb,
stone.” Noble generosity !
Melbourne Argus : —“The lobbying which
has become a public scandal in Victoria is simply
the outward and visible sign of the demoralisation
to which Protection invariably leads.” The
Argus evidently imagines that the person who
button-holes members in the Parliamentary lobby
is never, by any chance, an importer who wants
duties taken off, or a prohibitionist, or a person
who wants to raise the age of consent, or a man
who wants a billet, or anything else save an
utterly unprincipled Protectionist. A lobbyist,
by the way, is a man who goes to see a member
about a grievance, and it has never been shown
that he is necessarily any more scandalous than
the man who goes to see an editor about a para-
era ph.

“The Shop Girl,” which has succeeded
“ In Town ” at Sydney Lyceum, is (as The Bul-
letin mentioned on its Melb. appearance) the
work of a person alleged to he named Dam.
Judging hy the result, however, this paper now
concludes that Dam is merely a feeble alias, and
that the author’s name is really Blast. The “Shop
Girl ” is very much like its two predecessors, and
they were very much like it, and also presented a
strong resemblance to each other, and till three
were so much the same that they could hardly he
bold apart. The characters are nominally differ-
ent, also the songs and the high kick and the
tiling which Dam, or Curse, or whatever his name
may he, calls the plot, hut they alt convey the
same suggestion of the tired and morally-diseased
dude in “Lady Windermere’s Fan ” set to music.
Also, they all vaguely suggest a butterfly coming
home drunk in the small hours of the morning.
The semblance of a plot is based on the fact that
a shop-girl—name unknown, hut identifiable hy a
strawberry-mark on her left shoulder—who was
abandoned on a doorstep in infancy, litis become
heiress to a fortune of -JH.OOO.tOO. There are 400
girls in the emporium, and five different indivi-
duals, including it French nobleman, want to
examine all their shoulders at once. Finally a
girl with a mark on the middle of her hack so
nearly wins the prize that Moo ley, the fatuous,
red-headed emporium-proprietor, rushes her off to
a registrar and marries her on the spot. The pro-
ceedings are thereafter diversified hy music and
dancing, and the anguish of a love-sick
shop-walker with a stutter in his feet,
and hy the irruptions of an old lady in
a rainbow dress, and hy an ancient joke about
eggs, and other things, till, at 10.30, it proves
that Maud Hobson’s mark is in the wrong place,
and Decima Moore, whose mark is in the right
place, takes the pool, A number of other ladies,
whose marks have been inspected and found un-
satisfactory, are rejected at an earlier stage of the
proceedings.
* # •
The emporium in the first act is depress-
ing, and suggests that Ilooley’s stock is run low,
awaiting fresh shipments. Act 11. is full of dazzle
and brilliance, hut hy that time all the ladies’
marks seem to have been inspected, and a good
deal of interest has consequently worn off, and the
play begins to roll in its stride, and is badly
bumped as it comes into the straight with four
feet of water in the hold, and its legs evidently
giving out. Bradfleld, as the medical student, is
only a duplicate of Bradfleld as the captain in
the last production, and Monklionse as Hooley
(or Hooley as Monkhouse, whichever it may he)
lias no conspicuous points about him, Fred Kaye,
as a shop-walker i’u the first act and an ancient
Roman in the second, is the only person who
seems to strike a new idea, and Maud Hobson’s
song, "Found on a doorstep at half past nine,” is
about the only ditty which sounds reasonably
new. All the same. The Bulletin hereby pro-
phesies that the “Shop Girl” wdl do well.

Sydney Criterion, the home of high-class
comedy in the palmy days of B. and 8., re-opens
on Saturday in the old line of business. That
prince of managers and universal favorite, Mr. L.
J. Lohr, is the lessee. The company includes
“ Billy ” Elton, Geo. Ireland, Lachlan M’Gowan,
Westmacott, Mrs. Walter Hill, the beautiful
Miss Harrie Ireland, Miss Blanche Carlyle (of
“Charley’s Aunt”), and other good names. The
season commences with “The Guv’nor”—apiece
of the kind for which the Criterion used to he
famous, before B. and B. went wandering after
the strange society drama of the kind written hy
Oscar Wilde (now detained by urgent business in
a large stone building). Sydney of late has had
nothing but large, hulking drama, and various
kinds of variety, and there should he a great
demand for another season of good comedy—the
kind that has no woman with a barrowing past.

The two old variety shows at the Tivoli
and Empire are alive and well, but the attempt
to run a third one fell flat, and the ill-starred
enterprise dried up. There wasn’t enough of it
for one thing, and a very thin programme was
dragged out like a racked heretic to make it cover
the space, and the pianist was always threatening
to slide off a chair which was much too high for
him. If the show had lasted much longer he
would certainly have slid off on to the floor, and
perhaps humped himself fatally, so it may he that
its ending was all fur the best.

The heroic deeds of Private Rignold with
the British flag in the limelight have kept
“ Youth ” going for three weeks at Her Majesty’s,
and it is to he succeeded on Saturday hy that reli-
able old production, “The World Against Her.”
Said production isn’t quite so new as it was, hut,
having been made of good material at the start,
it will probably wear a little while longer. Never-
theless, it begins to assume a greenish aspect when
viewed sideways in a strong light, and to exhibit
snail-tracks along the seams.
*» * *
ITaddon Chambers’ play “ John-<\-Dreams”
turns out to he only a high-flown melodrama with
an irrelevant spicy episode wedged into the middle
of it. The heroine volunteers a confession that
she was an improper female in days of yore, when
she lived with an unvirtuous mother, now deceased,
witose conduct she “ gives away ” for the sole pur-
pose of remarking that “ we ought always to speak
well of the dead.” There the matter ends. An
aged parson betrays his feelings in a Look of
Horror for one moment. The next moment he
withdraws the Look unconditionally, and goes on
reverencing the young woman as usual, despite
her unpleasant Past. Also, the ardent lover
makes no objection to her Past, and her reason
for having had one at all becomes wrapped in
mystery. Haddon Chambers might just as well
have inserted the ghost of Coriolanns, with in-
structions to relate the story of OKI Mother Hub-
hard, and then vanish from the plot.
One week of “John it- Dreams ” was more
than enough for Melbourne, and on Saturday
“The Amazons” brought sunshine baok to the
Princess’s. It would seem that Pinero’s fanciful
comedy improves with age. Anyw’ay, the extra-
vaganza, of Messrs. Boucicault and Cecil Ward,
who now play their parts in a rather more comic-
opera spirit than before, lias improved upon its
original popularity. A patriotic Frenchman,
perhaps, might object to such a riotous caricature
as tbe Count de Grival if there were no Earl of
Tween wayes to keep the foreign nobleman in
countenance. Tween wayes, the hereditary ruin,
has crumbled a good deal since his previous
efforts on the same stage. He is gone over further
at tlie knees, and the blind staggers which his
family acquired in the Crusades threaten to double
him up forever in the course of a few more revivals.
Also his racial incapacity for tea and his high-born
dread of Hereford bulls are getting worse as time
goes on. Tweenwayes and the Count played some
of their peculiarities to elm gallery on Saturday, lint
with the best results. “The Amazons” travelled
like the whirlwind, although its charming female
interest is slightly weakened hy changes in the
cast. By the way, critical exception might he
taken to that carpet of fallen leaves in the sylvan
glade. In the first place they clearly haven't
fallen from the trees, which are still full of foliage,
and are only just beginning to take autumnal
tints. Secondly, the leaves are dry, find there-
fore out of accord with the pains in Cecil Ward’s
tummy. “ When the air is cold and damp, the
true De Bray must suffer cramp.”

On Saturday, the B. and B. Co. will try
yet another new show—“ The Importance of
Being Earnest.” This is understood to he a light
and cheerful comedy, hy the author of “ An Ideal
Husband.” In fact, it was written hy the late
Oscar Wilde, not long prior to that unhappy per-
son’s social decease, when dungeon shadows were
already creeping athwart his literary career, and
mental anxiety had sharpened his sense of humor.
** * *
“ A Sailor’s Knot ” crowds Melbourne
Royal every evening. All the melodramas produced
this season have caught on, hut the “Knot” got
such a firm grip of the populace that it might
have hung on for a month at least if other
arrangements didn’t compel it to shift. Mrs.
Bland Holt and the manager who hears her
name are responsible for the triumph of this par-
ticular festival. They occupy a strong position in
the centre of the plot, and hurl comic relief at the
surrounding sin and misery. When Joe gets
taken prisoner in France; his constant Margery
consoles herself hy sneaking certain despatches
from Marshal Ney to a cockney hoat-hnilder, and
when somebody announces Wellington’s victory
on the Field of Waterloo (which was quite un-
known by that name at the time) the historic
event is felt to lie merely an unimportant detail in
the lives of Margery and Joe, aforesaid. “A
Sailor’s Knot” will he followed, on Saturday
(10th), by “Saved from the Sea,” a drama full of
the odor of brine and entirely novel sensations.
In tliis Mias Elizabeth Watson makes her first
attack upon an Australian audience.

The Opera House show, which ranges
from substantial Larry Dooley down to a fragile
infant who twangs a banjo on rudimentary princi-
ples, is in a prosperous state as usual, whilst the
Oxford remains open without seeming to prosper
much. The varieties there are not feebler on an
average than other varieties that have made
money, but Melbourne doesn't particularly want
more than one joy of the kind, and Rickards sup-
plies the wanted one. The little Cambridge
Theatre is about to re-open for gospel services, and
a dog-show is now in full cry at the Exhibition
building.

The three brightest ditties sung in “The Shop Girl”
are annexations. Decitm Moore gets her “Di 1 Di! ”
from a modern outrage called “Go Bang.” The original
singer of “ Di ! Di !" was Letty Lind, or perhaps the fair
Letty picked it up at one of the ’alls; anyway she, with
her rather husky notes and better-trained legs, made a
gooii deal more ontof the number than little Miss Decima
could. Bradfield’s song, “Do not trust him,” was either
written for or interpolated hy Arthur Roberts in the
"Don Juan” burlesque, and Roberts sang it distinctly
better than Bradtield tn the opinion of people who have
heard both. Another rorty ballad, which Bradfleld does
no sort of justice to, is “Her gulden hair was hanging
down her hack.” This is a hackneyed music-hall pro-
duction only suitable for a woman. Alice Leamar
“ knocks ’em ” with “ Her Golden Hair,” and Priscilla
Verne, at Melb. Opera House, takes the shine out of
BradfleUFs mechanical rendering of it. Yet the mildest
suggestion that Bradfleld was not brilliant in this or many
other songs would he blasphemy just at present. Let a
clever comedian get hold of a very fat part, and for the
next few weeks he is a god. Ask Elton
i’liil. Greenwood, the Firm's secretary, was lately sent
to Denilionin on sick leave, and his employers’ kindness
is producing the best results. Tall Phillip has made flesh
rapidly In the calm, balmy retirement of the N.S.W. town-
ship. Friends will he glad to hear that his lung complaint
has been placed under timely arrest, and that he expects
to return to Melbourne anon with distinct signs of a Cor-
poration, and no traces of the cough which he took away
with him.

Adelina Patti has been singing “ Home, Sweet Home”
for 40 years, and it is still worth £1 a word to her every
time she utters it. Patti, however, is a rapidly waning
light. She meets with apologetic criticism nowadays.
One musical authority ventures to observe that despite
“rather well-worn high notes,” the diva “bids fair to
repeat her operatic successes of old in parts which do not
make any great demands upon the resources of a voca-
list.”
In her recent London appearance ns Trnviata, Patti
wore a perfect cuirass of (her own) diamonds- 3700 in
number, not one of which weighed less than 6 carats.
The total value was over £200,000.
Apropos of a recent Bulletin paragraph, Mr. 11. J.
Samuel), music-publisher, writes: "Nellie Stewart sang
A. VV. Juncker's song ‘I was Dreaming’ purely on its
merits. When ‘Ma Mie Rosette’ was to be produced in
Melb. Miss Stewart wanted a song to replace one written
by the composer of ihe opera for the second act. She
tried over several, and was leaving the stage at rehearsal
in disgust when, looking on top of the piano, she said,
1 By the wav, Caron, here is a song just come by post ;
let’s try it,’ It was ‘I was Dreaming.’ Miss Stewart liked
it at once. Conductor Caron did not; hut, anyhow, MissS.
said, • Let ns have a cadenza at the end ; ask Barker to
oblige with a harp obligato, and I’ll fix the words—
because, the second act of “Ma Mie Rosette ” being a
dream, it won’t do to give it away.’ Hence Miss Stewart
sang ‘I have been thinking.’ 'And that is why many
never knew she sing ‘ I was Dreaming.’ And that,
too, is why the publisher rlid not pay her a royalty. I
wanted to give Miss Stewart a diamond bangle, but
manager Musgrovesdd a royalty or nothing, and, under
the circumstances, I really felt hound to decline paying
royalty.”

That proviso in Premier Reid’s Land and In-
come Tax Bill which exempts from tax all incomes' arising
from N.S.W. Govt, debentures held hy people outside the
province mill/, is a horrible example of the worst jdnd of
Foreign-trade policy. With the income-tax at Is. in the
£ the result would he that the large foreign holder of 3£
per cent. Govt, debentims would get J 3 10s. per cent, on
his investment, while the one in the province would get
just, £3 6s. 6<i. Thus, if a N.S.W. institu lion put
£1,000,000 of its money in local Stale securities it would
get t!750 a year less net interest than would an English,
or Queensland, or Maoriland, or Chinese institution u tier
the like circumstances! The idea of offering special
advantages to the foreigner in preference to ids own
people is a piece of foolishness of which only the O. 11.
Keid kind of person is capable. Any individual with a
little more brain and a little less waistcoat circumference
would have given any possible advantage to the local
investor.
It would be a breach of faith to tax the divi-
dends of the existing British stockholder, so he isn’t to
he taxed. It would he mere foolishness to tax the future
British stockholder, for if he was offered the present rate
of interest, less income-tax, he would simply offer has
for the stock, and the result would be as broad as it is
long—perhaps a lit tle broader. Whether it is equally a
breach of faith to tax the dividends of the existing local
holder is not the question at issue. In the interests of
common sense he should be exempt now and for ever.
The amount to be raised hy a tax on the miserably small
amount of local divs. is next to nothing. And if Govt,
stocks were exempted they would become a favorite
form c( local investment, and that fact would materially
help along the time when N.S. Wales would he indepen-
dent of the British money-lender. Moreover, if they were
exempted from taxation the Government could sell new
issues at a higher rate, so that it would probably gain
almost ns much ns it would lose, •fhe Govt, stock-holder
does not require legislative discouragement, like the land,
holder ; he grabs no unearned increment; he doesn’t
block settlement; and he gets a very small rate of
interest. To exempt him from income-tax might not be
abstract justice, hut, at all events, it would be a wise
policy.
