Goldsbrongh, Morfc and Co. propose to recon-
struct by cancelling t wo-and-a-half millions of 5 per
cent, debentures, and issuing in their stead one half the
amount in 4 percent, debentures and one-half in dehen.
tures hearing interest at 3 per cent. This will make the

saving of over £37,000 a year. Under the 1803 recon-
struction, 10s. per share was written off. and another P's.
per share has since been called up. It is now proposed to
call up £2 more per share, and shareholders who pay in
full before the Ist October and then agree to write off
another 10a. per share, are to be freed from all further
liability in the matter of calls. Also, if the co. wants to
pay off its debentures at any time it has the privilege of
doing so hy paying £lO5 for every £lOO debenture. If
this scheme through it will probably save the good
old firm of G.M. and Co. from something mueh worse in
the future, hut, all the same, the eall will smash a good
many shareholders. This miserable country is full of
calls already.

The City of Melbourne Bank’s English creditors
propose a reconstruction scheme of their own. Its main
points are that the deposits are to be divided into A
debentures at. 4 percent, and B debentures at 2 per cent,
interest, so that the rate all round will be 3 per cent.’
instead of the present 4J. Further, the directors are to
be five in number, three elected hy the British deposi-
tors, one by the Australian depositors, and one by the
shareholders. And when the depositors run the show
and root out the doings of the old management, and make
calls to the amount of t’400,000 or so, and proceed to sell
up the management’s friends for their overdrafts, there will
be great doings. By far the b« st thing the shareholders
can do under these circumstances is to ask for a release
from further liabilities and hand over the whole hank.

A BEAUTIFUL COMPLEXION. ,
It has long been known to scientists that for
improving the appearance and beautifying the
face oatmeal is far and away superior to all other
foods, but it had one drawback—the tendency to
heat the blood. To overcome this defect the at-
tention of eminent scientists has been devoted to
the removal of this imperfection ; and now, after
long years of careful and di'igent research, art has
triumphed, and Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats is the
result, for, by skilful manipulation in the manu-
facture, all the heating properties have been ex-
tracted, so that now a beautiful complexion of
peach and ivory may be had by anyone who lhakes
a continuous use of Uncle Toby’s Rolled Oats.
Information. Also, what does the chairman mean by the
word "discharged?’'

The Investment Agency Co. of Australasia is
the latest British idea for exploiting these provinces. Its
mission is to raise large sums on debentures and other-
wise from tiie British public at a low rate of interest, and
Invest these borrowed moneys and its own capital in first-
class Australian securities, such as the shares of two or
three leading banks and insurance companies, municipal
debentures, &c. If well managed it will probably realise 6s.
or 10s. more per cent, on its investments than it lias topay
to its debenture-holders, and pay dividends out of the
difference. There is no end to the devices of the awful
Bull to make money, with the least possible amount of
risk and no hard work, out of these miserable “colonies,"
and until every kind of absentee landlord, speculator,
trader, mortgagee, and miscellaneous cormorant is hear ily
taxed for being an absentee, the eating-up process will go
on and on and on into the endless perspective of Hades.
• * * *
A Municipal Fire Insurance Bill has been intro-
in Maorilshd Parliament. It authorises each municipal
council to value all the buildings within its boundaries—
certain extra hazardous risks excepted—and then impose
an insurance-rate on the owners. Every owner whose
rate is paid will thereby be held insured for the ensuing
year to three-fourths of the value of bis property. The
beneficial feal ure of this scheme is that it will provide
cheap insurance by saving the heavy item of commission
to agents and much of the clerical expenses. And, on the
other hand, it appears to he utterly inefficient on two
points. A municipal valuation is generally very low, and
three-fourths of it will be a wholly insufficient pro-
tection ; if the scheme is to be put on anything like
an equitable footing it will be necessary to insure
up to at least nine-tenths of the valuation. Also, there
seems to he no provision for insuring the contents
of the buildings, and if a man has to insure his building
with the municipal office and his furniture or slock with
some private institution, the new scheme will only he, to
very many people, an aggravated nuisance. If the muni-
cipality was allowed, however, to compulsorily insure all
the insurable property within its limits up to 90 percent,
of its estimated value, then the experiment would he
worth trying as another move in the direction of Stale
and municipal socialism.

The Melb. directors of the Proprietary mine
who rushed to Broken Hill at great speed per special
train immediately news of the fire reached them, took
a supply of “chemicals,” with which to subdue the fire.
Said chemicals, when overhauled by practical people at
the mine, were found to consist of a quantity of com-
pressed carbonic acid of the kind used by serated-water
manufacturers, sufficient, when liberated, to fill a fairly
large room with the gas. Whereat much irreverent mirth
at the mine, where the practical off! 'inis had made
arrangements to produce the carbonic acid gas (by roast-
ing coke and limestone) at the rate of many millions of
cubic feet per hour. All sorts of inventors have
besieged the Proprietary management with suggestions
of all degrees of absurdity for the extinction of the fire,
hub the directors, with their bottles of carbonic acid, are
conceded to have taken the cake.
Which circuitously recalls that at the time
of the Broken Hill water-famine an anxious mother, who
had read the newspapers and pictured her son at Broken
Hill dying a lingering death from thirst, sent him from
Adelaide a 400-gal. tank of water—cost of carriage, £8
something. The same quantity of water could have been
bought at Broken Hill for t 3.
Not much is heard as to the probable origin of
the (ire at the Proprietary mine. But as the afternoon
shifts in the open cut had lately been working by the
light of bonfires of old mine timber, the new regime of
economy having brought about a reduction of t he electric-
lighting, and as the lire is held to have started from the
surface, the matter, so they say at Broken Hill, does not
leave much room for doubt.
• • * ♦
The fire, which broke out in the Southern end of
Block IX, which is the southernmost of the B.H. Proprie-
tary Co.’s three leases and adjoins Block 10, was first seen
by a party of watchmen. At hast the smoke was seen,
as a matter of fact, nobody has seen the fire; for,
notwithstanding the columns of high-flown news-
paper description, it is extremely doubtful if, at any
lime, it has done any more than smoulder. It
was the smoke which, finding no easy outlet, filled
the workings, and repulsed the men at IHe begin-
ning. Afterwards, of coins?, the workings had to he
kept closed in order to shut off currents of air. At the
most, it is doubtful if the lire more than charred a few
timbers. The general-manager does not believe a single
stick is burned, through; and Dr. Robertson shares the
opinion. And even then it burned in a part of the mine
which had been practically abandoned so far as sloping
was concerned—the "creepiest” section of the whole line.
Those who got their ideas of the fire from the
daily newspaper reports are to be sympathised with. One
imaginative writer likened the spectacle to a volcano in
active eruption. This, however, was the exact position :
a large and irregular excavation ; from cracks here and
there either smoke or steam issuing, but now here much
more than from the kitchen-chimney when the breakfast
fire is being lit ; a few men tearing down the sides of the
excavation, and throwing dirt over the cracks; a few
more holding hoses, allowing the water to fall into the
bottom of the quarry, whence it soaked into the mine.
Sometimes smoke and steam would almost entirely dis-
appear. Then the suectacle was no more exciting than
the falling of a stream of water out of a hose into a deep
hole.
Eight years ago, when the use of soft Oregon
timber for staging the Broken Hill mines was introduced
by Mr. Patton, many prophesied disaster. But it was
pointed out that with the rough timber the square-set
system would be impossible, and the obvious merits of
that system in a broad lode carried the day easily, lu
practice, too, the soft wood has been found to possess
many advantages. It gives slowly ; the hardwood
would snap. In a “creepy ” mine any sudden movement
of this kind could only end in disaster. Ten-inch blocks
of Oregon have been found compressed to less than half
that size. The real danger from fire began with the opeu-
cuttiug, which has exposed the upper timbers.
Suppose a fire gets hold of the sulphides ! They
will burn by themselves. A kiln of them would take
about four months to bum itself out.
Just before the tire there were 4070 men etn-
ployed tm the Broken Hill mines ; of these 2200 were iu
the B.H. Proprietary’s underground workings, smelters,
and mills, and 700 in the quarries. The Central employed
400; the South, 270; Block 14, 225; Block 10, 100;
British, 85. But Block 14 has its smelting plant at Port
Adelaide, and the B.H. Proprietary has six smelting
furnaces and a refinery at Port Pirie. The fire in Block
11 threw 1300 of the Proprietary’s men out, and caused a
loss of between £3OOO and £4OOO a week to the S.A. rail-
way revenue.
Some timorous Broken Hill shareholders Were
alarmed when they were told that American mines had
burned for nine years. But the cases are very different.
There the mines were from 1000 to 1500 ft. deep, worked
out almost from Ihe surface, and not closely mullocked
up. At Broken Hill few slopes are as yet below 350 ft.,
and most of the worked-out ground is filled up, not with
timber only, but with debris. For eight years either the
general-manager or the mine-engineer has been an
American, using American timber after American
methods; the first month after an American ceased to
hold either office this trouble arose. But, of course, it is
no more than coincidence.

At the Australian Widows Fund meeting, Hume
Qook, M.P., objected to the appointment of ex-Premier

not the point. The question is—« as it or was it not good
business policy to appoint Mr. Patterson under the
circumstances?

The striking of the lode at 1000 ft. in the New
Golden Gale mine, Tas., gives that mine some 400 ft. of
backs on an enormous reef. The little island is famous
for its big mines. Tire new G.G. has only called up Is. 3d.
on its 32,000 shares of 6s. each, and has since 'BB got
68,0000 z. gold from 62,000 tons stone, and paid in divs.
£82,400, equal to £2 11s. 6d. per share. It is equipped
with a 40-head battery, which has been closed down a
good deal of late lor waul of water.

The action, Dalrymple v. Prince of Wales and
Bonsliaw Co., in w hich plaintiff was twice knocked out
by a lower Court Judge, and once by the Full Court, lias
now been abandoned. A Privy Council appeal was
threatened, but plaintiff has let tbe time for entering the
appeal go by. The co. lias won half-a-dozen similar
actions (re alleged illegal forfeiture of shares), and the
shareholders now hope there is a chance of divs.

A Coolgardie wire : “ Good judges express the
opinion that Hie Marilana is a greater mine than the
Boulder." So far, the public have not quite Hie same
confidence. The market value of the Boulder would buy
several Marilauas. It is another instance of what may
happen to a mine in W.A. Thousands of Mari tuna shares
have been forfeited, and many times during the Uet 18
months it was regarded a hopeless concern.

Big managerial changes announced at Cool-
gardie. Oats and Brookman, the Great Boulder and Lake
View (tosses, have resigned, and il is rumored that Aarons
is leaving the Londonderry also that Col. North cabled
Zel). Lane to report on the present condition and future

The Bulletin has something to report of the
Londonderry. The crushing-plant has arrived on the
ground, and no one seems to know when it is to be
erected. The gold chute at the 100 ft, level is 5 to 6ft.
long, ami very patchy at (hat. If the level had been
driven 10ft. higher the chute would have been missed
altogether. It came up from the floor, and before it
reached the reef it turned over. Ttie reef has been driven
on about 400 ft. at the 100 ft. level, but outside the chute
it is not payable. There seems a very had time ahead of
the Londonderry, and Col. North will still have an oppor-
tunity of demonstrating his philanthropy.

West Australia Goldfields Co. (London) de-
clared a S7i per cent, div., added £50,000 to reserve, and
carried forward £40,000. A big feat, accomplished not
hy producing gold, hut hy selling alleged gold-mines.
The W.A. Israelite Bay “rush” seems to be
another ghastly hoax. It has caused upwards of 2000
men to roam aimlessly about in different directions.

The Melb. gun-frauds case has achieved
one pathetic incident, anyway. While the con-
victed Abrahams brethren were dodging their
victuals in the gaol infirmary, a young man, who
was making enquiries into their business on behalf
of their London prosecutor, was caught in the act
of sneaking a gun for himself. The poor devil,
having promised to plead guilty next morning, got
himself bailed, out from the watch-house, slunk
quietly into his home, and committed suicide in
the bath-room.
When a diligent and honorable physician
of keen medical instinct passes away the world is
much poorer for Ids loss. Such a man was Dr.
Louis Ralston Huxtable, of whom a portrait is
printed elsewhere in this issue, and whose death,
last week, was caused by pneumonia following on
diphtheria, probably contracted at Sj'dney Chil-
dren’s Hospital, of which he was an honorary
medical officer. Dr. Huxtable’s funeral was
attended by about 00 of his professional brethren,
including the leaders of the profession.
The wide circle which has so often and so
long laughed with Livingston Hopkins (‘•Hop.,”
of The Bulletin), and which, on the 7th ultimo,
congratulated him on his 50th birthday, now
sorrows with him at the loss of his kindly and
estimable wife, whose remains were, on Wednes-
day of last week, interred at North Shore ceme-
tery, in the presence of many friends. Mrs,
Hopkins, who was a native of Ohio, and came- tc
this country with her husband from America
years ago, leaves six children, and was at the time
of her death in her 47th year.
Premier JReid, who has just been presented
with an expensive gold chronometer, to make up
for the watch he lost on last election night, says
lie will always wear a 12s. fid. article at future
political demonstrations, or when going home
from Parliament at a late hour. Considering G.
Reid’s copious figure this is a prudent resolution.
G. Reid’s watch, when he carries it in his waist-
coat pocket, is situated at the remotest part of his
vast protuberance, and a thief might take it out
of the suburbs of his frame, as it were, and get
away with it long ere the rest of G. Reid could
overtake that part of him where the robbery was
being committed. When the Premier is in Pitt-
street, Sydney, with his watch in his waistcoat
pocket, it is, in a manner of speaking, about as
remote and unprotected as if it was hanging on a
tree at Parramatta.
Old John Kneebone, who was one of the
six miners who lost their lives in the Eldorado
disaster, was at one time a rich man. The Knee-
bone mine, Reedy Creek, was named after him,
and he made £120,000 or =£30,000 out of mining
ventures. He was not, when lie died, worth more
(financially) than his fortnight’s wages. The other
victims were in much the same case. About the
most pathetic will on record was that which one
of them, Dawkins, scratched on his billy ;
“ Am getting faint, no air. God protect me for the sake
of my poor children and my wife. Lizzie, look aftir them
and bring them up good. The-money I have in my box
and bank is to lie divided with the little ones. Make the
best of what. I have saved. God will lie good to them.
Kiss them for their poor father's sake. 1 forgive all my
. Love to all that are dear to me. Good-bye, my
dearest children. The money you will find (£2) in the
lumber-room." On the bottom of the billy was written
"Saturday, 6 a.m. Will you find 12 in lumber-room,
near the leg of the hunk, hasr end. Love to all. Write to
my poor mother and wife.— C. Dawki.ns."
He died of suffocation, and was found with his
will beside him. Two of his mates died in the
same fashion. Kneebone almost escaped, but a
mass of timber fell on him, and broke both his
legs, and the avalanche of sludge overtook him.

lienee.
The new number of the Dictionary of
National Biography contains an article on Sir
J. F. Palmer, who was a cordial-manufacturer in
Melbourne, and the first president of Vic. Legis-
lative Council. His great-uncle was Sjr Joshua
Reynolds, the first president of the British Royal
Academy.
General Dodds is such a big gun that one
wonders what the devil he does in an Australian
galley. Well, The Bulletin hears that Fanre,
Ribot, et cie., are as much afraid of him as the
Directory was of Bonaparte when it sent him to
Egypt, in the devout hope that a pyramid, or
sphinx,' or something would crush him flat as a
pancake. The shuddery Boulanger is not yet
forgotten.
Cabled that, in a letter thanking the elec-
tors of South Down for the support accorded him,
Dr. Rowan (formerly of Melb.), the unsuccessful
Conservative candidate, attributes his defeat to
“ignorance, superstition, and illiterate brute
force.” Dr. Rowan is a parrot-faced gentleman
of high repute as a gynecologist. He 'was long
resident physician at Melb. women’s hospital, and
is the husband of a Vic. heiress.
Trenwith, M.L.A., now unhappily almost
blind, when he made bis great speech on the Yic.
Tariff Bill, brought, down to the House articles of
local manufacture with which to illustrate certain
points. When he came to the question of axles
he picked up the wrong article and exhibited it,
until the guffaws of one of the Opposition mem-

the blind politician afterwards took his critic
aside—by the ear.

Mr. H. Palmer Abbott,
who would probably get most
votes as champion N.S.W.
Rugby footballer, was born
at Brisbane (Q.) in ’72. He
started the game in ’B7 as
a member of the Grammar
School first fifteen ; and
afterwards became captain of
the school both in football
and cricket. He quickly took
a prominent place among the
best football players of the
northern capital, and in ’B9
he played as wing three-
quarter back for Queens-
land against the Maori and
N.S.W. teams. In ’9O he
came to Sydney, joined the
University team, and estab-
lished his reputation as one of
the best all-round players
that ever donned a guernsey
’tvvixt goals. His team won
the premierships in ’9O, ’9l,
and ’93; was second in ’92 ;
third in ’94; and is third on
the list for the current sea-
son. Abbott’s position in
these matches was always
centre three-quarter back.
He was elected captain in
’93, and has held that posi-
tion ever since. He played
with Queensland against
N.S.W., at Sydney, in ’9O ;
with N.S.W. against Queens-
land, at Brisbane,' in ’9l;
captained N.S.W. against
Maoriland in ’93 ; and cap-
tained N.S.W. against Cen-
tral Queensland on 6th inst.
Abbott prefers wing three-
quarter to any other position,
and thinks that “a lot of
attractiveness is taken out of
the game by the system of
wing-forwards now in vogue,
which is rapidly ridding the
game of hard-working for-
wards. The system,” says
the ’Varsity hero, “incites
forwards to loaf with a view of waiting an open-
ing for a bit of ‘ gallery-play.’ It also encourages
off-side and an unfair interference with an op-
posing team’s half-backs—faults that can only be
prevented by strict and arduous umpiring.”

Premier Kingston was born to trouble.
When high up the political ladder, he figured in a
hot and costly love-affair; then came the absurd
duel, in which Kingston at least showed manliness
doubtfully possessed by his latest enemy. The
S.A. Premier sometimes ascribes all his troubles
to the fact that a lawyer’s clerk once committed
suicide against the office-door of Kingston and
Hall, and stained the floor with blood. Since
then his luck has been out.

his years. Since his earliest days Piddington has
sincerely believed himself to be a genius, which

• Another Tyson
story, “ I was school-
mastering in a small Q.
township, at the tail-
end of the 80’s, when
the long millionaire
dropped in in his usual
promiscuous, forgot-
my-Ciird-oase style. I
was just then rounding
up all the people who
had a spare pound for
subscriptions to the
school-fund, and I
thought Mr. Tyson a
most likely mark. So
I interviewed him. I
said, ‘Good day, Mr.
Tyson. I’m the school-
master here, and I’m
trying to collect the
local fifth part required
by Government belore
it Will call tenders for
the improvements ne-
cessary for the school.
Will you give us some
help ?’ ‘ What are the
improvements going
to be ?’ he asked. ‘ A
play-shed, a bell, and
a fence,’ I replied.
‘ Ha, ha,’—and it was
a horribly sari astic ha,
ha —‘a play-shed, eh?
You wanta play-shed,
indeed J Do you think
it p, proper thing to
accustom our young
Queenslanders, our
future carpenter-, bi.il-
lock-drlversj, shearers,
damainkers and theii
wives to be unfit for
work when the sun is
too hot or the weather
a bit wet? Nonsense!
And a bell,’ he con-
tinued, indignantly,
‘ what rubbish ! Set
them up with a bell!
What for? To have
them at school in
time? Haven’t they
got tiie laughing jack-
asses to waken them
up every morning as
they have me for many a day? Why not
give them valets or lady’s-maids and alarm-clocks;
Children ought to be taught the value of punctu-
ality in such a way that, bell or ho bell, they would
be there from a sense of duty, not only to others
but to themselves. Now, the fence is the only
really sensible thing of the lot. It will teach them
the rights of property and the illegality of trespass.
I will give you this (.£5) for the fence, and just
put it down from a friend—l don’t want my name
to appear. Good day.” The Bulletin believes
most of tliis story, which is characteristically
Tysbnian. The old man’s fetish is self-reliance;
also, when he gives away money, which he does
in plenty, you don’t read about it in the papers.
When G. Republican Dibbs was journey-
ing per night-train to Tam worth and the elections,
he discovered that his was a top berth in the
sleeping-car. Much troubled, he asked the con-
ductor to try and arrange wit h some gentleman to
change berths. A gentleman did oblige. “ Who
has been so kind?” asked the knight of many
damns. “ A Daily Telegraph reporter,” replied
the conductor. “First d thing the Tele-
graph ever did for the country,” exclaimed Dam
Chicago.
Albert Calvert, mining-promoter, de-
scribed as “the eminent Australian explorer” (!!!)
was banqueted at the Imperial Institute (to what
base uses !) the other day, and from pictures in
the Daily Graphic and New Budget, the Lon-
don correspondent of S.A, Advertiser gathers
that “the service of plate presented to the unspeak-
ably modest person aforesaid was of an excep-
tionally sumptuous and solid character. But
why, oil why, are the teapot and coffeepot, not to
mention the tray, surmounted by models of an
obese and long-necked goose ? Is the noble bird
intended to be typical of the promoter of, or the
investor in, Westralian mines, or was some subtle
compliment to Albert Calvert, M.1.C.E., in the
subscribers’minds ? On second thoughts, I have
come to the conclusion that the aforesaid bird
must be a swan and not a goose. The larger and
less savoury fowl figures, I believe, in the Westra-
lian coat-of-arma. ”

POLITICAL POINTS
Seven Hebrews sit in Vic. Parliament.
Pauperism would be abolished at once if
the Legislative Council could get a dividend out
of the job.
The N.S.W. general-election has resulted
in Premier Reid having fewer open enemies and
more unreliable friends.

why should he be appointed to N.S.W. Leg.

More J.P.-isra. It appears that the other
day a police-opposed application for a hotel-license
was granted by a northern N.S.W. bench by two
votes to one—one of the two magistrates con-
stituting the majority being the father of the
applicant!
Parkes’s fierce charge against Reid of
44 interfering with the liberties of the people,”
brings to memory the shameful enactments pro-

after lunatic O’Farrell’s shooting at Alfred of

Whispered that the Separation feeling in
Central and Northern Queensland will be tested
at next election by each elector receiving a ballot-
paper with the question 44 Are you in favor of
Separation-Yes or No?” The Referendum to
the front again.
The old law which required members of
N.S.W. Leg. Council to be sworn at the com-
mencement of each new Parliament was a most
salutary one. Were it in existence now, at least
four who hold the 44 honor ” and 44 the pass ” would
be officially effaced.
To appoint ex-Treasurer See as the leader
of the N.S.W. Parliamentary Protectionist party
would be ridiculous. Mr. See is respectable, but
he is dull and snapless as a canned oyster. When
choosing between the Devil and the Deep Sea,
always select the Devil.
The late N.S.W. elections were held to
decide 44 the clear-cut issue of Upper House
reform.” And now the Beer interest and the
cold-tea saints are busily engaged in proving
that the only issue ever thought of by the electors
was Bung versus Lemonade.
Under the current estimates, the total
expenditure on Vic. Parliamentary Library for
the year is to be .£2237, of which only .£450 goes
for the purchase and binding of books. The
salaries of the librarian and his assistants amount
to <£1787. The librarian at Melb. Public Library
gets £419 a year, and has a colossal and con-
stantly-growing collection of books to look after.
The Parliamentary Librarian gets .£546 a year,
and has practically nothing to do during half the
year.
Sydney D.T. berates the S.A, voter
generally because he 44 has not realised the dif-
ference between the importation of Asiatic goods
and the importation of the races who produce
them;” and then weaves wonderful bosh which
ends in asserting that, when the S.A. voter gets
wisdom, 44 he will not be found in opposition to
trade-reciprocity proposals.” Implying that the
Ereetrade D.T. itself is in favor of reciprocity.
Very queer. For a Freetrader is bound to be 44 in
opposition to trade-reciprocity proposals,” because
—(1) they break the nose of his economic god ; and
(2), even if he wants to reciprocate, he has noth-
ing whatsoever with which to reciprocate— i.e., if
he is honest and 44 makes it ” Ereetrade.
On the Vic. railways, despite the great fall
in revenue, the working expenses have been
reduced, since the close of the Speight era, from
70 to 59 per cent, of the receipts ; the actual cost
of running trains has been reduced from 3s. 9d. to
3s. 2d. per mile, and the cost of the railway work-
shops has been cut down almost one-half. The Age
was mainly responsible for the ejection of Speight
and Allison Smith, and these figures were" the
main reason why Speight and Smith’s libel-actions
against that paper came to so melancholy an end.
Money is so scarce in Melbourne just now that a
jury feels that almost any kind of language i-
justifiable when directed against men of larg-
spending capacity.

circles just now is Mr. G. H. Reid, writes The

Dibbs and Protection ” used to be the bogey, but

really to be one of Reid’s M.L.C.’s ? If so, the

N.S.W. Assembly will again pass the land-
tax and income-tax, and the Council, it is stated,
will again throw out the land-tax—on the alleged
ground that any exemption is unjust. It may
also throw out the income-tax, alleging that the
exemption is fixed too high. Then Premier Reid
will stalk off to England to arrange in that sodden
land for an alteration in the Constitution.

THE COMPARATIVE MOOD.
SNOOZER : " This beer is bad—very bad.”
BOOZER : “No sunk — hie! —thing as bad beer. There's good beer and
better beer.”
London Times regrets —and the faithful
“cable” re-echoes its regrets:—“ that -Premier
Reid’s majority is not sufficient to enable him to
lispeuse with the support of the Labor-party.”
Many people rejoice that the Labor-party’s posi-
tion is strong enough to enable it to dispense with
the support of the Times.
Many N.S. W. employers are starting to
discharge men by scores, and some by hundreds.
This, no doubt, partly owing to the immediate
prospect of their business being rendered unre-
mnnerative by the alteration in the tariff ; but
partly (in at least some cases) as the result of
a National Ass. conspiracy.
Public opinion will not be satisfied by the
release of the surviving Mount Rennie boys.
Justice, however tardy, must be dealt out in the
matter of their trial. That the public may be
fully informed as to the nature of that hideous
farce, the admirable series of articles in the Aus-
tralian Star should be republished in pamphlet
form.
a Bill legalising marriage with a deceased
husband’s brother has passed the Maoriland House
of Representatives. It went by a large majority,
but with an utter absence of enthusiasm. Plenty
of politicians feel like marrying a deceased wife’s
sister —especially her younger sister, but a de-
ceased husband’s brother Bill is not a person to
enthuse about.
The latest. Working men in Walgett
(N.S. W.) district, writes a Bulletin correspon-
dent, are now told that if known to possess
electors’ rights they will get no shearing or other
employment locally if the managers or big dum-
mies can prevent it. The idea being that as
under the secret-voting system one never can
be sure how a man will go, it’s best to make
certain by knowing that he doesn’t vote at all.
One or two of Q. Labor-leader Glassey’s
speeches on his return from America gave hope
that he was getting sense ; but the hope was soon
damped again. But for the accident of being first
in the field, Glassey would never be even thought
of as leader. He is honest enough ; but vain,
intemperate, injudicious, and intolerably long-
winded. His errors this session are more flagrant
than ever. Either Reid or Dawson would make a
much better commander.
“If,” writes Anarchist Andrews to] The
Bulletin, “Government honestly wishes to re-
move some of the obstacles placed during the past
century in the way of the people’s prosperity and
independence, why not facilitate the task of those
who wish to go upon the soil and draw their
subsistence therefrom ? We have a system of
4 miners’ rights,’ the working of which is well-
understood ; why not declare the remaining lands
inalienable, and issue land-occupation rights in
respect of them on a similar basis —shortly stated,
4 title to occupy for personal use, existing and
ceasing with such bona-fide use ’ —which would be
an incalculable boon to many and a hard knock to
the sharks?”
A would-be candidate, interviewed a well-
known light of the Sydney boxing-ring and told
him he would give him ss. each for 50 signatures
of voters in a certain city electorate to a requisi-
tion asking him to stand for Parliament. 44 It’s a
bit out of my line,” said the apotheosized pug. —
“you’d better go to Soandso, who will fix it for
you.” Soandso agreed to 44 fix it,” and produced
the needed signatures within 24 hours. He had
simply gone down to Devonshire-street cemetery
and taken 50 names off the gravestones. 44 I
really hope,” said the candidate while writing out
the promised cheque,” that, these men are all
right and will really support me.” “Nothing’s
absolutely sure in this world,” replied Mr. So-
andso, 44 but I think I may venture to bet that not
one oi them will work against you.”
A former representative of a Sydney con
stituency, now elected for a N.S.W. Norther*
district; 44 1 will never again stand for a seat
near home. My house was never free from in-
trusion, and every butcher and baker and candle-
stick-maker demanded my custom with, as it were,
political menaces.”
For a temporary messengership in Maori-
land Parliament buildings during session, there
were 130 applications in two days. The State
will never be debarred from extending its business
through lack of persons willing to enter its service
At present, mainly because of party government,
the business extends but slowly, and the greatest
enemy of a political party is the necessity for
supporting its own support - >-

“The Queen’s Own” and “The Pope’s
Own” two antagonistic civil service secret
societies^ —have collided down South. In keep-
ing with tradition, anonymous threatening letters
have been flying round. Both societies are
influential in their own tin-pot way—an ex-
Premier and prominent politicians being members
of one or the other.
He had two scrutineers (paid by himself(
at the booth, and they were both electors; also,
they both swore to vote for him, their generous
patron. But when the poll was declared the
candidate found that not a single vote had been
cast for him at that booth, and that no vote was
recorded as informal. Each scrutineer had
decided to “sell” his employer, and each had
reckoned on the other not being such a thief as
himself. It was as good as a play.
Important Questions to the Public.
“Are you subject to indigestion, pain in the
stomach, flatulency and headache ? ”
If so, take Warner’s Safe Cure, which never
fails to promptly relieve these symptoms.
“ Do you have pains in the right side, or under
shoulder blade, a bitter, oily taste in the mouth, a
furred tongue ; specks before the_ eyes; suffer
from giddiness, nausea and drowsiness?” Sure
signs that the liver is inactive.
Then use Warner’s Safe Cure, which is a power-
ful liver invigorator, and sure cure for these ail-
ments.
“Are you subject to rheumatic pains, and swel-
lings of the joints ? ” An acknowledged result of
impurity of the blood.
Then resort to Warner’s Safe Cure, the only
reliable remedy, as experienced by thousands in
all countries.
“ Is your breathing laboured, have you lost the
florid colour, your appetite, flesh and strength ?
Do you suffer from palpitation of the heart ? Are
you wakeful at night and drowsy during the day ?
Do your ankles or limbs swell ? Is your eye-sight
failing ? Is there an occasional dull, heavy pain
over the loins, and deposits in the urine? ”
If so, the kidneys—these important organs—are
complaining. No time should be lost. Procure
and take Warner’s Safe Cure. A remedy which
never fails in these diseases, as proved by the
testimony of thousands.

There are two distinct stories of the King-
ston-Sparks encounter told in Adelaide. The
democrats have it that Sparks came quietly up to
Premier Kingston and set about him from the
rear, whereas Fat Man represents the aggressor
as a chivalrous land-grabber who would scorn to
win first blood on a foul. The Bulletin puts
very little faith in the fair-play of a man who goes
forth in cold blood to horsewhip somebody who
has spoken slightingly of him. As a rule, the
revengeful person makes up his mind to
1 administer a sound thrashing ” in the easiest
possible way, without risking the conse-
quences of a preliminary announcement. Sheriff
Boothby, who was with Kingston when the
latter was assaulted by Sparks, was utterly
unequal to the occasion, and a narrative of the
little scrimmage published in America would
cause disgust in the breast of every citizen.
Boothby’s duty was to draw his revolver, fire at
Sparks and hit Kingston, and then shoot care-
lessly at the whole crowd, muttering between his
clenched teeth : “ How now, Woolloomooloo
Jack ! I’ve struck your tracks at last!” A great
occasion lost.
• # •
Rumored that Victoria’s coming Governor
has stated in private letters that he intends to put
his foot down on indiscriminate entertainment,
such as has hitherto been the rule at Govt. House.
Two “ state ” balls a year, closely resembling such
functions at Buckingham Palace, and to be taken
quite as seriously and with as much preliminary
prayer and fasting, is all Melbournites, not abso-
lutely within the charmed circle, may look forward
to during the Brassey regime.

dinner.” Some papers may get ahead of the
Herald for general news, but for enthralling
high-society information old Garrany is easilv
first.

Sec her tripping ’cross the street—
Swagger stockings, tiny feet !
Well she knows her ankles neat,
Or she wouldn’t show ’em !
But her sister —she whose shoes
Quaintly flop, like small caimes !
Watch her try, by cunning ruse,
Skirts to keep below ’em !
Thus it was since Time began,
Since Eve came to solace man.
Since, when nymphs for Paris ran,
Male things stood to con ’em.
Surely in those halcyon days,
Ere girls took to wearing stays,
Figures were beyond all praise,
Or—they’d had more on ’em !

* • •
The Hopetouns, no doubt, will be really
grieved at the death of Mr. Tom Manifold, He
and all his clan were prime favorites with the
Countess, who singled out the younger branches
as the only local juveniles worthy to share in the
sports of “ Lord Hope, if you please.” Her lady-
ship’s last gift to the little Manifolds was the his-
toric goat-team and shay, which was to have gone
home with the original owners, had they not dis-
covered, at the last moment, that “William” and
mate would be slaughtered on board ship, and not
allowed to set foot on British soil. Whereupon
they were packed off to Camperdown.
* # #
Actang-Gov. Madden was on exhibition at
a recent Melb. Salvation Army fandango, and
delivered himself of the depressingly dignified
speech that has become habitual with him since
Ids elevation. He also asserted that he had been
in receipt of two letters from persons who
besought him not to degrade his gorgeous and
almost Royal condition by attending Salvation
Army demonstrations, and lending his glittering
countenance to the big drum and its blatant asso-
ciates—vulgar persons who disseminate the Gospel
in back lanes. His Vice-Excellency took his cor-
respondents very seriously, and was at great pains
to point out that his conduct even at a Salvation
razzle was carefully controlled so as not to com-
promise Her Gracious in any way. This is true.
Chief Justice Madden, in his gubernatorial
capacity, patronises the theatre regularly, and is
so extremely careful of the vice-regal dignity that
he never goes out for a drink between the acts,
but sits simmering through the long waits, with
the burden of his unwonted magnificence heavy
upon him, staring blankly at the curtain with
eyes that never deviate, and with self-conscious-
ness oozing from every pore of his carefully
cosmetiqued person. Dignity is Madden’s strong
point—he is clothed with it as with a boiler-iron
night-shirt.
• * *
Victoria is suffering from aggravated hero-
worship. Acting-Gov. Madden writes letter after
letter of fulsome adulation to men whose heroism
consisted in doing brave deeds as though all in the
day’s work. The effect will be that at the next
mining accident men will be hesitating whether it
wouldn’t be better to die with their mates than
live to receive such pumped-up epistles.
# m m
That divorce-case, perhaps more talked-
about and written-about than any other for 20
years, remains in abeyance through the advice
of the lawyers, who hold outno hope of the con-
summation desired by both parties.
• * #
At a Melb. branch-meeting of the A.N. A.,
last week, an illuminated Certificate was presented
to Justice Williams with the hope that “ the time
was not far distant when the Association would
be able to congratulate his Honor upon his eleva-
ting to the highest judicial position in the colony.”
Either a wild utterance, or an inspired hint that
the gorgeous Madden expects to represent Vic-
toria, or Australia, on the Judicial Committee
of the P.C. in London. Or, if neither of these
things, it, was intended as a suggestion that
Madden would oblige the A.N.A. by sending
in his resignation at an early date. They can’t
be looking forward to his sudden decease. In the
first place, Chief Justice M. is a very healthy
potentate, bred to stay, and in the second place
nobody would like the world to lose him in his
waxed and golden prime. He is much too amusing
for that.

So desperate are things, getting in Bris-
bane, that single chappies, of the bank-clerk
brigade, are taking lessons at the Technical Col-
lege in tailoring and trouser-making.
• # #
The burning question of the place where
Gov. Brassey is to land, and the road by which he
shall enter Melb., is still unsettled ; it has proved
too weighty for Acting-Gov. Madden, who has
passed it along to the Cabinet. The seaside
suburbs have displayed so much feeling upon the
momentous point that it lias been suggested the
Sunbeam should come to anchor somewhere in the
Gippsland lake-i —whence Brassey could proceed
to Melb. by rail. But, in that case, civil war is
to be feared between the rival towns of Bairnsdale
and Sale. On the whole, it might be best in the
interests of peace were Brassey to land somewhere
outside Victoria and walk to the nearest railway-
station. No such interest about a distinguished
arrival’s landing and route has arisen since they
brought Deeming along from Westralia.

* * m
The other day quite a pretty little woman,
with a plump face and fuzzy hair, promenaded
the main walk of Brisbane Botanic Gardens in a
really nobby pair of rough serge bloomers. Mem-
bers on their way up to the House stopped to gaze
in paralytic astonishment at the seductive waggle
of a shapely pair of calves, such as even a dancer
might envy. Luckily for the business-paper of
the Council, after about half-an-hour she got into
a cab and drove home.

Bucolic Kanakaland M.P., welcoming
Gov. Norman to open the local “show ” the other
day: “In short, in the long line of Governors,
there’s not one as liever did as much for the
colony, not one as—as —as was hever so much
beloved—not one as Was hever—hever—a—a patch
on Sir Hantiny Musgrave.” (Smothered laughter
and cheers from crowd.) The Governor turned it
off nicely : “My eloquent friend made an obvious
slip, but, after all, perhaps the eulogy of my pre-
decessor was not misplaced. Sir Anthony had
done as much for the country, was as well-
liked ” “ Not ’im !” interrupted the enthu-
siastic legislator. “Good fellow in his way, but
not a patch upon this exhibit, eh, Sir ’Enry?”
(slapping him familiarly on the back). “We love
you, my boy. Three cheers for Sir Hantiny ’Enery
Wylie ! ” Then the crowd rolled on the ground in
ecstasy.
* * *
The most conspicuously be-diamonded
people at the conversazione of the Royal Colonial
Institute the other evening, writes The Bulle-
tin’s London correspondent, were the Victorian
contingent. Mrs. Rupert Clarke had a show
worthy of a Bond-street jeweller ; Mrs. Everard
Browne’s brilliants were the cynosure of neigh-
boring eyes ; Mrs. Cruickshank’s ornaments were
a perfect blaze, and another lady, the spouse of a
racing and mining man, was also resplendent.
These were the most conspicuous, but others, per-
sonally unknown to me, carried enough wealth
about them to occasion the remark that things
cannot be so very bad in some Australian circles.
» * #
Gaiety-Girl attractions, according to one
parson, consist chiefly in “ unsavory jokes and
liberal displays of anatomy ” —which is a lot truer
than his corollary that “a tide of nastiness is
swamping our national life.” Savoring of the
unsavory is an older institution than the parson,
and is no more likely to pre-decease him than to
culminate in that universal lubricity predicted by
the man with the long upper-lip every time he
gives to an “objectionable print’’ the minute
scrutiny which it receives from nobody else except
the more frankly prurient person against whom
he is continually taking up his parable. Man-
kind is no “nastier,” here and now, than it has
been always and everywhere.

Mr.. Gilchrist, the Sydney man recently
released from a Westralian prison, after doing
three months of an latterly undeserved three years’
sentence for alleged -“obtaining,” was directly
assumed to be guHty because temporarily unable
to prove his innocence. And it’s so, nearly every
time, despite the hoary lie to the contrary. Let
British “ fai-r-play ” once get you into the dock,
and you’re pretty certain to get the boasted
“ benefit of the doubt ” —against you !
Dear Bulletin, —lie that famous law of
Strzelecki quoted the other day. Strzeleoki asserts
that “aboriginal women, after intercourse with
Europeans, lose the power of conception on a
renew d of intercourse with males of their own
race.” The which so-called law is pure, unadul-
terated bunkum, and its complete disproof can
be seen any day in any aboriginal camp in
Central and Northern Queensland. I have con
versed with scores of men who know the blacks
well, and they all receive Strzelecki’s law with a
burst of laughter. Had Strzeleoki said the half
caste progeny are much more numerous than the
aboriginal, one could cordially agree with him.—
P. Luftig.

Various parties haggled over this document
in the law courts, and by the time they had
done haggling, and the Full Court had finally
upset the decision of the inferior court, there was
no estate left. All Thomas’s accumulations have
been swallowed up by the lawyers. Thomas spent
over 40 years in building up his little pile, and the
legal fraternity dissipated it all in just about 40
days. It is a very big estate indeed that can
stand the expense of an enquiry ,to find out who it
belongs to. An ordinary property disappears
under such circumstances like a pound of sugar
stricken by a waterspout.

Here are some actually existing relation-
ships completed by two Tasmanian families—the
names are false, but the exact state of relationship
is as here set down. Also, each separately new
family has now a nursery attached, and if the
progeny intermarry with any like recklessness or
ingenuity, what sort of a maze of consanguinity
will be developed ? Imagine, first, two families
named (say) Cox. Also, imagine another family
named (say) Box. The Coxes with whom we
have to cfo are (say) John and Julia, first cousins,
These marry. The Boxes with whom we have to
do are three brothers (say), William, Robert and
James. Of these, James has married altogether
outside of this calculation, and actually had a
grown-up son, James, jun., before the problem was
hatched. Well, John Cox married his cousin, Julia
Cox, Julia thus never changing her name. Then
William Box married John’s sister, Emily
Cox. Whereupon Robert Box married one of
Julia Cox’s sisters, Edith Cox ; and James Box,
junior, married another of Julia Cox’s sisters,
Caroline Cox. So : John Cox is husband and
cousin to Julia; brother-in-law, in two different
ways, to both William and Robert Box ; brother-
in-law to his own sister, Mrs. William Box;
brother-in-law to Mrs. Robert Box ; brother-in-law
and unole-in law to Mr. and Mrs. James Box;
and, as he is the brother-in-law of his own nephew-
he is evidently his own nephew. Similarly,

The Late D. HUXTABLE.
A small Vic. boy, convicted of attempted
arson the other day, when asked why he tried to
burn down a neighbor’s cottage replied, “Oh!
you can get cheap firewood for your mother.”
That is the sort of boy who will drift into politics,
and want to build .£700,000 State railways for the
purpose of improving the value of his 40-acre block
away out-back.
Scandal even in a deaf and dumb asylum !
The officials of the local institution in Melbourne,
and many professional pietists connected there-
with, have been consternated by reports of giddi-
ness amongst the inmates. Male deaf mutes, it
appears, have been secretly visiting the female
quarters :
The proposal to locate Melbourne’s new
cemetery at the pretty village of Frankston, 27
miles from the city, is being violently opposed on
the ground that its location “would be incon-
venient to people desirous of visiting the graves
of friends or relatives.” The present writer would
suggest, in a tentative sort of fashion, that this
might be one of the best points about the arrange-
ment. Most of the people who pervade ceme-
teries don’t go to visit the graves of their own
friends, but merely wander among the tombs out
of a ghoulish love of mounds and epitaphs, and
to spell out other people’s inscriptions ; and these
morbid individuals require discouragement. There
are so many of them that when the real mourner
goes to visit his lost relative’s resting-place he has
often to wait until some complete stranger has
done with it. How bad this tendency towards
graves is in some people was shown in the evid-
ence at a Dunedin (M.L.) divorce-trial a few years
ago. “We were married,” said the plaintiff, a
girl-wife of 22 years, “on the 28th June. It was
a cold day and raining heavily. After the mar-
riage we went for a walk in the cemetery.” Ever
since then The Bulletin has been of opinion that
a cemetery should be as inaccessible as possible,
so that no ghoul can take his bride there on a wet
day to catch a lifelong snuffle among the ghosts
and wormy things, and to get rheumatism in the
cold draught that blows round the marble slab of
John Jones.
* « »
* One of the managers of a Melb. refuge
recently wrote to the papers acknowledging
various gifts which, he said, enable his proteges to
“lie down with at least the comfort of a full belly
to keep company with a sad heart.” A neat bit
of honest old Saxon, the like of which one rarely
meets nowadays.
• * *
In Samoa there lives a haughty potentate
who periodically sends away his elder boys for a
week to live—wash, cook and make their own
beds—in the stable at the end of the family five-
acre allotment. The boys’ bedroom there is right
over the horse and cow stalls, and looks down upon
the pig-sty and the fowl-roost. At the week’s
end the banished youths receive from their parents
a formal invitation to dine. They formally
accept. Then, arrayed in evening-dress, they
come down the ladder from their room, pick their
way out of the stable, past the pig-sty and the
hen-roost, and over to the house of their host and
hostess. Formal greetings are exchanged, and a
formal dinner is solemnly gone through. “ What’s
the object of all this d d fooling ? ” once asked
a fellow Samoan. “Aw,” replied the exiled
potentate, “it is necessary to keep one’s sons
thoroughly in touch with the formalities of polite
society. We hope one day to be able to—aw —
leave this beastly country.”

Up to a month or two ago the Melb. up-
holsterers used to gee old hair teased at the
Emigrants’ Home and the Benevolent Asylum at
a cost of 2d. per lb. Lately, however, the gaols
have reduced the charge to l|d., and at the pre-
sent time command a monopoly, while a few
dozen decent old men and women are thrown into
the streets simply because the semi-oharitable in-
stitutions can’t afford to keep them doing nothing.

The other day, when it was raining heavily
and an icy wind blowing, and all was desolation
and slush, a picturesque procession went along a

From Cosmos —Falk Photo.
Miss PALOTTA.
main Melb. street. There was a cheap little
hearse first of all, with two black, bony horses
plodding along towards the cemetery. Next
there came one mourning-coach—empty. Then
came the rest of the show—one cab with a well-
known fat politician crumpled up in the corner
and fast asleep. His immense head hung one way
and his hat another, and his two huge, pendulous
knees shook like jelly with the motion of the
vehicle. His mouth was open, and a fatuous smile
covered his countenance. The crowd on a passing
tram-car caught sight of him and raised a wild
“boo-hoo,” but the blubbery mass only fell to-
gether a little and bobbed its hat dismally at the
crowd. And as the cab went by, the crowd on
the car could hear issuing from it a nasal sound
like “ gr-r-r-r-r-r-r-r r-r-rmph ! ”
# * *
During ’94, 956,840 “franked” letters
were posted in Tasmania. The rottenness of the
system was revealed lately in the course of some
correspondence between the warden of a country
municipality and the Treasurer. Recently, too,
a large package containing washing or drapery
lay in Hobart P. 0., in full sight of the public. It
was addressed to a country lady, and franked by
an hon. M.H. A, Funny stories are told of the
abuses of the privilege, such as invitations for a
party being sent post-franked whilst the M.P.
whose stamp was on the letters was touring
across the Straits. Some members, too, effect a
great saving in telegrams by using the wires gratis
in their private business.
* * *
At Slowbart recently, a defending solicitor
was questioning a publican, witness for the pro-
secution. “You drink, don’t you? Were
you ever out of your mind through drink?
Then he struck a snag. “When did you have a
drink last?” The answer came pat: “At two
o’clock this morning with you in the
Hotel.” Cross-examination concluded.
# * *
The newest thing to be seen in Melbourne
streets is a very smart “ dog-cart ” driven by an
exceedingly massive blonde German lady, whose
driving costume consists of a long, bright-green
Melton coat with two rows of “penny” buttons
down the front, and a three-quarter bell-topper of
same shade of green, with a bright scarlet wing at
the side. Running through the front of the bodice
of this showy “rig-out” is a colossal gold dagger,
topped off with opals and diamonds.
Two Northern undertakers, who lately
took expensive lessons in the art of embalming,
proceeded to practise their new accomplishment,
on a nigger’s corpse. It was agreed that the
coffin should be stacked away for a certain number
of days, when the embalmers were to be present
and open the casket to see how the poor clay was
keeping. But, urged by curiosity, one of the
ghouls repaired alone to investigate on his own
account before the day fixed. He opened the
coffin to gaze upon the subject, and, “ horrible,
horrible and most horrible,” the defunct nig. ex.
ploded —in the interests of Cremation.
« . * •
Mrs. Paxton, who died at Glebe Point,
Sydney, last week, during an operation, was the
only daughter of the late Joseph Paxton, of Hill
End mining fame. The deceased, a most charm-
ing woman, was until some few years ago the
wife of a popular and influential clergyman, whose
name she dropped after their divorce (at her suit).
# • #
During a recent municipal election in a
Melb. suburb, one of the candidates (a mighty big
swell) was vainly importuning the land!ady"of a
small hotel to vote for him. Her objection was
that she had no one to relieve her at the bar. The
difficulty was solved by the candidate uncoating
and going behind the bar himself, while the
liberated landlady went along to record her vote
for her pro. tern, barman (or one of the other
fellows).
DREAMS OF BEAUTY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When day is gone, and ere the veil of twilight
Has fall’n on earth awhile,
I see her in the sunset’s crimson glory—
A ripe and joyous smile.
Upon the trembling wavelets dance the moonbeams
In throbbing cadence sweet;
And white upon the shimmering waters twinkle
j. . Her silver-bangled feet.
The shrill-toned creatures of the darkness falter,
To listen in delight
Her song of love, vibrating through the whispers
Of never-silent night.
When through the creeping grayness of the dawn-
ing
The reddened daybreak shows,
Upon the limits of the land of morning
Her passioned presence glows.
Dawn passes; blatant day glares unrelenting ;
The sensuous vision dies :
Yet in the deep-blue dome of Heaven lingers
The memory of her eyes.
Vic.
C.P.R.
He had no burning words to tell
The fervor of his flame, poor lad ;
And she—which proves it’s just as well—
Could not have heard them if he had.
Not for her ear the liquid lute,
No serenade could win him grace ;
They both were deaf and both were mute,
And yet, ’tis hinted, he must face
A rousing breaoh-of-promise case !

Wear, Men’s and Boy’s Clothing, Boots and
Shoes. Riley Brothers still continue the system
Originated by them 30 years ago of sending £5
Drapery Bales and 32:5 parcels, carriage paid, and
a large business is now done daily in
postal orders from fid. upwards. One of Riley
Brothers' latest investments is the purchase of the
entire stock in N. 8. W. of Dr. Scott’s celebrated
electric corsets and hair-brushes. Eminent doctors
say, and thousands of testimonials aver, that the
corsets are a splendid remedy for ladies’ ailments,
especially backache, and the electric brushes give
instant relief in eases of sick headache, neuralgia,
sleeplessness. The corsets are just like ordinary
stays, and perfect-fitting. Sizes only 24 to 2‘J
inches ; price, Gs., post free. The brushes are

for himself. The use of Amykos has proved to bo
very important to this end, by its great sanitary

value yourself, never be without this excellent pre-
paration.*

August 6, 1895.
My Dkab Moorabinda—
With a good floor and gallery attendance, and
dashing decoration, Tempo ball was really a great
success this time. Hut I wonder when ball-com-
mittees will learn that the spikey jama is not a
desirable adjunct, unless placed lift, above high-
dancing mark. To trim your pillars low down
with this dagger-edged plant threatens all sorts of
mishaps, from a gouged eye to a torn petticoat—
yet decoration-committees invariably include it in
their beautifying schemes. The dais and cosy
corners were handsomely upholstered—the supper
arrangements (cafe style) were a triumph, and the
decorations of wattle-blossom pretty enough to
excuse a lot of the bad “ puitry ” which is heaved at
this bilious, indigenous shrub. The music was
fairly good, the waltz-tempo suiting the Gaiety
style of ballroom dancing, which suggests the
gyrations of a sheet of newspaper in a brickfielder
rather than the dreamy style of rotating. Sydney
girls do not exactly crave to have the nicely-
tailored and haw-haw G. G. actors as waltz-
partners—they haven’t foot enough for these
johnnies. A “waltz” with a Gaiety “boy” is a
frantic rush down the room—a pause of four bars
while the inexperienced lady gets her wind—then
up the room, and repeat. It looks like the way
they train horses for a fire-engine.
Talking of dancing, as gallery crowds are a
fixed institution now at public balls, people will
have to be more careful how they trip it. To look
down upon a square dance and see some of the
performers “balancez” while others “chassez”
hardly looks as if the Sydney hop-and-skip pro-
fessors had reason to be proud of their pupils.
The Tempe ball was a very “ society ” and well-
groomed spectacle, with the Acting-Governoress
iu black and green and diamonds practically
demonstrating the advisability of having your
corsage cut V-shape dorsally instead of square if
you happen to incline to a fine figure. Miss Dar-
ley's dress suggested a good crushing—all yellow.
Mrs. Hutton, in good-looking black with biting
diamonds, carried a large bouquet of daffodils.
Mrs. Prendergast (lion, sec.) in pale blue, and Mrs.
Brennan (hqn; sec;) in pale-pink set one another
off admirably. Mrs. G. Russel I-Jones wore a
delicate brocade of up-to-date cut; Mrs. Rennie
was in sunbeam-yellovir silk, and so was Miss
-»l‘Donagh—but the evening dresses were rather
hustled by the fancy-set girls, who absorbed most
of the attention.
Mrs. Prendergast’s Gainsboro’ set bore away
the bell. The dress was a cream satin with Maud
Millett fichu of white chiffon—big black velvet
hat trimmed with feathers and pink roses, black
mitts and a black wand with pink roses and
ribbons on top. The Misses Fitzgerald and Mias
Nellie Hill all looked ch inning in the picturesque
garb (they were poudre, too), and the last-named
demoiselle’s skilful handling of her Aaron’s rod
must have made her partners rise up and call her
blessed. In one set a girl with a Maypole nearly
poked her partner's eye out—though, perhaps, she
did it on purpose? According to an old wives’
fable, you have to marry a person if you blind him,
and the general matrimonial outlook just now its
rather desperate. Mrs, Cape’s Shepherdess set
was rather lame about the petticoats.
The interest of Mrs. Harvey’s Army and Navy
set centred in the men who wore the gaudy garb
of (polite) butchering and buccaneering with good
wall-poster effect, but the ladies wore ordinary (in
one or two cases very ordinary) evening dress with
a slight draping of tricolor ribbon. Mrs. Delo-
hery’s “ Winter ” set was white with scarlet birds
and white wands topped with scarlet, and a “ pink
and blue ” set (more suggestive of delf than china)
and a floral set were the rest of the "fancy”
goods. One misguided but comely maiden, not in
the fancy sets, came as Lydia from “Dorothy,”
evidently imagining the occasion was “costume ”
throughout. However, I don’t suppose she
cared ; she possessed a very neat ankle, and per-
haps only felt grateful all the evening that her
petticoats were a good half-yard shorter thin any
other giil s on the floor. Only one Gaiety girl
turned up, so far as I saw—Laura Kearney, in
t rat outre black gaberdine of hers.
Very few flowers were carried at either the
Tempe or Masonic ball—the reason being: that the
frost and the drought combined have made flowers
a. golden luxury in Sydney lately. Roses a shil-
ling to eighteenpence apiece—daffodils threepence
each—carnations and gardenias almost as dear as
diamonds. No wonder the fascinating Gee Gees
aren't thrown enough verdure to make a Sunday*
size button-hole for the call-boys.
The big Masonic ball in aid of the Freemasons’
Benevolent Institution, held in Sydney Town
Hall on the 31st ult., was a scintillating spectacle
—the decorations running to scented fountains,
palms and other Sybaritish trimmings, although
bare walls would have passed unnoticed on the
occasion, seeing how gorgeous in themselves are
full-fig Masonic fetes. And the last ball was no
exception. The Grand procession, with the arch
of steel and all the rest of the historical pageant,
was certainly the gaudiest shilling’s-worth the
Town Hail galleries have got this season ; and
when the .lengthy Grand Master Abbott (they
always choose a large-size G.M., I notice—some-
body with a good physical frontage, as one might
say, so that the Masonic jewellery and fancy-work
will have plenty of shop-front) appeared with his
dress-suit almost hidden by his jewelled and
sparkling regalia, the mi se-en-scene was admirable.
George Kignold himself couldn’t havestaged things
better. Ihe steel arch was beautifully done,
though I must confess I had misgivings as to how
the shorter “chapters” would manage to preserve
the arc when such sons of Anak as the Acting-
Governor and the Gram! Master had to pass under
it after the style of “Oranges and lemons.”
However, nobody had to save his scalp by ducking.
Supper was comprehensively served at the lavish
hour of 10.30, and the music of the N.S. \V. Artil-
lery band, kindly lent by Major-General Hutton,
made poetry of the dancing. Altogether, the
Masonic ball - committee had reason to be
gratified.
There was every sort of dress on the floor, from
the Acting-Governoress’s magpie blend and the
Grand Alistress’s stand-alone black-satin with the
usual glittering fixings, to a lady in coffee-
color nun s-veiling with a cluster of silver
flowers, which she had evidently got off a wed-
ding cake. Some of the dresses were rather
daringly V-shaped, and I wondered (when the
transferment of Masonic regalia took place)
why those Alasons whose aprons decorated the
mure decollete didn’t hang the little blue hand-
kerchief-sachets round the ladies’ necks instead of
their waists? But, after all, women occupy a
back seat on these occasions—they always seem to
me to take up the position of the homely hen at
Alasonic outbreaks—because it is then that the
soberly-clad male bursts forth into the gorgeous
coloringwhich the barnyard monarch has appropri-
ated since the Garden of Eden. At such times you
can t help thinking that perhaps men have made a
mistake in leaving all the finery to women. Nature
never works that way—or only in rare instances—
and yet men disregard her example, and wear all
the snuffy hen-colors themselves, while women
have all the rainbow tailings and cockadoodledo.
Perhaps fine clothes are really at the bottom of
the Women’s Suffrage agitation—like the cuckoo
(I think it was a cuckoo) which stole a peacock’s
tail and fancied itself a lawn-bird on the strength
of it ! D
Apropos of balls, in justice to the Tempe ball com-
mittee-men I wish to say that they never requested
the patronage or presence of Alayor Lees at their
dance, and a recent report I made to that effect
was incorrect in detail—it was to the suburban
R.G. charity ball that Sydney’s Alayor refused
the light of his spectacles, though experts say the
result would have been the same anywhere.
Alayor Lees isn’t a ball-goer or giver. i have
only once seen him and his mayoress on the bees-
wax. and the rigid manner in which he confines
his official attention to, say, drainage questions
and that soit of thing, strips our hitherto rather
“smart” mayoralty of the lively air with which
ex-Mayors Bnrdekiu and Manning invested it.
\ tl ,(Jll ßht the most blood-curdling thing about
“The Shop Girl,” at Sydney Lyceum, was the
slip-slop get-up of Mr. Hooley (Alonkhouse), pro-
prietor of the Royal stores. Air. AI. evidently
dressed the part from English— not Australian—
models, but if “the drama’s laws the drama’s
patrons give,” Air. AI. should certainly evolute
into more dazzling linen and a white handkerchief
instead of a green one when essaying the part.
From a local d.o. point of view there couldn’t be
too much pompousness and collar about “ Alister-
ooley.” Convincingness was not with Air. Fred.
Raye s attire as Aliggles, either—who ever saw a
shop-walker in light trousers and a sac coat selling
perambulators to the aristocracy? None of the
men turned out smartly in “The Shop Girl,”
barring Bradfield—and he owjht to know whether
his clothes fit him or not, so I hear. Charles
Ryley sported very so-so apparel as the millionaire
—indeed he almost appeared to have conceived the
part a la Jimmy Tyson, who is probably the
most-plainly, though not the worst, tailored mil-
lionaire on earth. But the girls were in full
feather. Aliss Aloore, as the Shop Girl, hadn’t
the chance to what you call “dress,” but her
black silk frock, with vviiite collar and call's,
and shoes with big buckles, were quite the most
becoming things 1 have seen her wear. The second
act, “Flower Girl” gown—an impression of red
and green and spangles—was too bizarre to exactly
suit Aliss Aloore’s Quakerish style, though the
whole effect was charming, naturally. Decima
made quite a hit with “Hi Hi”—a triple encore,
with floral trimmings, following her arch render-
ing of the ballad of the ballerina.
Grace Palotta, in the meaningless part of Lady
Uodo, sang a delicately quaint song about the
“Naughty, naughty man in the moon,” which
was loudly re-demanded, and rewarded with some
garden-stuff. Allte. I*. sings it while wearing a
very dignified garment of white brocade, with
pearl and silver trimmings, and quite a Parisian
head-dress a huge half-star of “diamonds,”
punctuated vviih purplish roses. A posy of purple
flowers and ribbons went with the turn-out, which
was in the very best taste, and also presented a
novelty that women .’with fiddle-string gullets are
sure to copy slavishly—l mean the deep band of
the same material as the gown, and embroidered
with Pearls and silver, like the corsage
which Aliss Palotta wore round her neck. Lady
Dodo’s first gown, of pale lemon-color silk with a
white crepe underdress and a few artistic touches
of black, was a deserving garment, especially
when topped off by a piquant black velvet hat
and feathers. Apropos of Li Palotta (who is
married, by the way), a love-lorn youth wrote to
her for her portrait the other day—enclosing his
own as a guarantee of good faitli ! As he is a
married man, this line of proceeding was—er—
indiscreet, to say the least of it.
But, then, married men are not always careful.
I hear of a well-known muchly-married man—a
pillar of the church, and all that—who recently
sent a fatherly reply to a “distressed widow’’
advt. and signed his full name with a flourish !
While men persist in spilling documentary evi-
dence all over the place in this foolish way, the
adventuress of melodrama who gets board and
lodging merely for the trouble of showing her host
a little pink note now and then will never be
wiped out.
In the “Shop Girl ” (which wouldn’t sound half
so broadly vulgar if they all rattled through it as
Brad Held does) Maud Hobson wore one gorgeous
garment as Ada Smith, which was picturesque
enough at all events—mustard-yellow satin with a
cape of yellow lace and braces of brown fur, the
corsage further adorned with clusters of yellow
roses—just the sort of frock a shop girl would
select for a “trouser’’ out of Hooley’s emporium !
It was topped with a rather taking thing in
coal-scuttle bonnets tied under the left ear in a
big bow and trimmed with a yellow feather, but
though Miss Hobson is always radiantly lovely
—a big woman never looks so well in a Scoop
bonnet tied under one ear as a small woman,does.
The latter always reminds you of a baby, but the
former wildly suggests the lady who borrows
little Mary’s hat to go out for the beer. Madge
Rossell (she is only 18) scores her prettiest in the
charming Japanese dance —the chrysantheme
draperies of blue satin and scarlet exactly suiting
her. Her light and dainty dance on a sprained
ankle was a marvel of pluck. Mrs. Phelps sported
the most blasphemous garment in the play—a
loud-swearing combination of yellow, with streaks
of gravy red—as if somebody had been trailing
the grilled steak over the butter.
Sydney Amateur Orchestral Society hadn’t a
very enthusiastic turn-out for its concert on
Bank Holiday, in Sydney Town Hall. I thought
to hear Hazou applauded when he got into the
box—seeing it was his first appearance after his
voyagmgs—but not a heel-tap came from the
assemblage which nearly filled the big hall. Cer-
tainly the gifted conductor skipped into position
in a hurry, to play the Anathema ushering
in Acting - Gov. Harley and two daughters,
backed up by Captain Hangar—but there
was time for one round of applause. I
have often noticed that when an audience may
he generally described as having a flower in its
hair it usually isn’t cordial. Perhaps because its
clothes are too tight. The S. A.O.S. played
delightfully on th • whole, an 1 the highly-polished,
all-wool items contribute;! at intervals by Nellie
Young and Mr. DM gar 'cellist Strauss made excel-
lent diversion. The Eroica symohony was the
Society’s great card, and though the well-meaning
orchestra got in a few ear-achey effects here anil
there in the difficult work, it was still a success oil
the whole and most gratifying to Signor Hazon,
and to Rivers Allpress, who coached the society
in this great work during Hazon’s absence. The
Peer Gynt suite and the Tannhanser overture
were also very taking items on a most classical
bill. The S. A.O.S. is nothing if not classic. No
composer who isn’t positively blue-mouldy need
apply.
The second of the Esty-Marsh operatic recital?
in Sydney Centenary Hall, on Monday, would
have been a great success had manager Sykes
exercised his gift of accompaniment-playing for
the benefit of the co. and not left Herr Vollmar
to do so much. Accompanists are born, not
made. The operatic portion of the concert was
“ Maritana,” and Miss Esty scored a great success
with the “Scenes that are Brightest” recitative
and aria, but the debonair Mr. Marsh made the
hit of the evening with “In Happy Moments.”
For his modulated and melodious rendering of
that sterling old chestnut he was rewarded with
whistles from the gods and weird cries of “’cor !”
from the front seats, to which he was obliged to
respond. Ida Osborne made a welcome appearance
with the E.-M. Co. Her smooth voice and roving
dark eyes were great additions to the programme,
which also included Her Vollmar, who played a
'cello solo exquisitely. A very good house turned
up on both the first and second nights.
The distinguishing characteristics of the Mad-
den entertainments, ntMelb. Govmentouse, writes
my Melb. correspondent, are the air of friendly
hospitality which pervades them and the de-
butante show on the dais early in the evening.
The group of maidens, clad in white, standing
apart, undergoing informal inspection, strongly
suggests to the cynically-minded hong's picture,
“The Slave Market.” On Thursday evening last
the display of “comers-out” quite eclipsed that
at the previous dance. Some fourteen girls, each
more or less bewilderingly pretty, went through
the ordeal, and were rewarded by being intro-
duced by the hostess to the smartest and
most eligible youths in the room. In my
opinion, the “beauty cake” for that evening
would go to Miss Leura Lugg, an exceeding
dainty maiden in a Watteau-shepherdess costume
of white brocade flowered over with pink and blue
brightened up with silver sequins; to Miss Eva
Woolcott, radiant in white satin, lilies-of-the-
valley, and lace; and to Miss Singleton, a pretty
girl with what Ouida calls “tawny ” hair and a
cast of face adored by Burne-Jones. Judging
from the women present, it was easy to see that
during the coming season, which promises to be
very gay, there will bo a good stock of beauty to
draw on for smart functions.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.
Speaker Abbott has been elected president
of the N.S. W. Art Society, defeating Mr. Blox-
some, who has held the position for a year past.
The Speaker is not known to have manifested any
spacially-artistic sympathies, and the only reason
fur his appointment seems to be that he is a man
of political arid social influence. An organisation
which cannot keep within its own ranks any
honor which m iy attach to its presidentship only
invites the contempt of the public.

SPOUTING NOTIONS.
The first forfeits, just declared for Caulfield
and Melb. Cups, show very few non-contents of note.
N.S.'V. is still well represented iti Caulfield Cup by
Vanitns, Solarium, Alahee, Nobleman, ami Division—and
the absolute winner will likely spring from t his quintette
if it can beat the Victorians, Idolater and Tullamore.
• * * *
It is on the cards that the winners of both the
Caulfield and Melb. Cups were seen out at Moorefleld
(N.S.W.) and Caulfield (Vic) last Saturday, competing in
races the aggregate value of which amounted to £l5O.
The speedy and staying Vanitas, who won the Tramway
Handicap (6 fur.) at Moorefield, carrying Bst. 91h., is, as
yet, not nearly ripe, and with 7st. lOlh. he should run
very well in the Caulfield Cup.
J * » • *
Delaware, Mahee, Ronda, Coolalta, and True
Blue-witi do battle in Melb. Cup, and theirmost dangerous
opponents from the Southern province may lie found to
be Dreamland, Palo Alto, and The Admiral. The latter will
likely run better in this race than in Caulfield Cup, despite
the fact of his being now absolutely first favorite for that
raoe. Dreamland, Mirtculeux. Marshal Ney, Tullamore,
Eumarrah, Taurus, War Dance, and Carlton (Hales) ran
in ttie Balaclava Stakes (one mile), at Caulfield, and have
all accepted for the Caulfield Cut'. It one of these is to
win this valuable prize it may lie Tullamore, the property
Of a well-known Vic. surgeon, “Mr. T. Naghten.”
Eumarrah, Merman, Taurus and St. David are also ac-
ceptors for Melb. Cup. It looks as if Scobie, with Dream-
land, is going to adopt the “training in public” tactics
which were so successful with Patron last year. Palo
Alto, a Robinson Crusoe horse, is already whispered about
as a most likely outsider, and if he represent, Mr. Purdies
instead of Portsea he should be well worth watching.
*•• » ♦
Pilot Boy, Oxide, Milkmaid, Sunburst, Res-
ponse, Euroka, and Voluey at first sight seem the most
leniently treated in the Epsom (Randwick) Handicap.
Volney and Euroka, the second and third in this raoe last
year, may fill the first two places on this occasion, though
the best of Air. Forrester’s, probably Alilkmaid, looks
dangerous.
Oxide is even better treated in the Metrop., and,
if reserved for the race, should about win. Blue Cap
appears to have an undeniably good chance.
« » * *
Laundress, who will be ridden by Joe Tomlin,
is highly thought of for Caulfield Cup, hut Bst. 71b. is a
lot of weight for this speedy mare, who is more at home
over a short course than a distance.
* • * •
A compromise has been arrived at between the
two leading S.A. racing clubs and the bookmakers,
whereby reputable layers can do business on the “ tote
commission ” principle in the stands on S.A. racecourses.
The clubs have, in the opinion of many, made a serious
mistake.
• « • *
No democratic “sport ” was sorry to learn that
the V.R.C. dropped coin over its precious complimentary
meeting to Lord Hopetoun, the one and only meeting of
last racing year which didn’t show a profit to the club.
The farewell folly wou d have paid expenses hut for the
desire of Hopetoun’s worshippers to make it “worthy of
the occasion ”in the wav of stakes. The result proved
that they over-estimated the occasion’s worth by £460
11s. 6d.
• • • •
Unless anything happens to him in the mean-
time, Trenton's son, Dreamland, will likely remain
favorite for Melb. Cup until the dav of the race.

should Daimio win the Caulfield Steeple 1
