W. R. Wilson didn’t, at time of writing, cotton
to the idea of closing the St. Alban’s lottery on Oct.
24th. Geo. Adams, who stands to lose all the cost of
advertising in addition to his com. if the sweep doesn’t
fill, is quite agreeable to the October date for drawing,
but the owner of the ’osses feels concerned about the
valuable racing engagements which fall due at that time.
F 1 t instance, if a Timhuctoo subscriber, who h id changed
his address since ha posted his pound, were to draw
Havoc, what would he the propier tiling to do with the
noble animal on behalf of its new owner 7
• • ♦ ♦
Gibson Brothers, the twin owners of Daimio,
are generally understood to race their one horse purely
for the sake of sport and the stakes. However, a lady
correspondent has it that “they wager, hut when the
expenses to jockey, trainer, &c., have been paid, the rest is
sent anonymously to t he hospitals,” which, if true, is about
the oddest tiling iu charity schemes that Tub Bulletin
has ever struck.
* • « «.
Boxing has knocked out its own popularity in
America, and Corbett is going to take all the dollars he
can by exhibition-spars, hall-punching, &c., before he
risks meeting Jackson in the ring. Returned Australians
say that Corbett, now 23, has only to wait two or three
years to walk over Jackson, so quickly is the darkey age-
ing.
* • • * •
At Sydney Amateur Club, on Monday night,
there was a good boxing programme. Barron and Mason
opened with a willing four rounds ; next Ned Burden
boxed Richie ; Burden is in grand buckle, and his match
with Funnel!, next Monday night, should prove an exhibi.
tion. Mick Dooley and Tom Taylor, both fat as aider-
men, boxed four rounds of circus and smiles which
brought down the house ; the priestly face of Dooley,
and the wink ’av ’im, and the way he “shammed
frightened” and yelled “police!” was worthy of Elton.
There was a hush of excitement for the evening’s event,
the 10-round contest between Tweedie and Foster.
The moment the boys shaped, Foster worked Tweedie,
with deceptive, cat-like movements, into the corner of
the ring and ttien let go witli a bang. Tweedie covered
his vital spots with Ills arms and rushed his way out.
This first rqjmd was fast and fierce. The second round
was at lightning pace, and the hoys utterly exhausted
one another. The third round began like the second, lint
before it was over Tweedie was too exhausted to go on,
and the referee awarded the match to Foster.
• * * •
If Corbett and Fitzsimmons do meet on 31st
Oct. at Dallas, Texas, it will be the biggest boxing event
the world has seen. It is to come off in a huge amphi-
theatre capable of seating 50,000 people. Tickets one,
three, five and ten pounds each, with a few boxes at a
century. ’Tis a strange world. Who would have believed
it possible, a few years back, that the scarlet-headed
lamp-post—who could he seen any Sunday morning,
dressed-in-his Sunday best, walking down Glebe Point
Hoad (Sydney) alongside his wife, with a youngster in his
arms, would turn out so brilliant a gladiator '?
* * » *
Notwithstanding his many defeats. Creed on
must have fared well in the States to enable him to refuse
to meet one Butler on the score of “purse not big
enough.” Such is luck. Mick Dooley, who could thrash
a paddock-full of Crcedoits, would jump at the chance of
a smaller purse than Creedon refused.
• « * «
What an unfortunate fellow Dooley has been 1
None at his weight game to tackle him, he has been the
tryer-out for such cattle as Peter Jackson, Paddy Slavic,
and Joe Goddard. Dooley never went into the ring with
the idea of standing up to the black man, but his fights
witli Pad((y Slavic and O ddard were touch-and-go affairs,
Dooley being beaten in the end by sheer strength and
weight of metal.
« « • «
The next Australian Eleven goes under the
auspices of the Melb. and Sydney Associations. Should
Giffeu try to dictate (as he did last time over brother
Walter), he himself may be left out, as he is not in Eng-
land the invaluable man he is in Australia. But the
omission of Giffen would, no doubt, mean the withdrawal
of the whole S.A. brigade, which would never do.
Many of the “certainties” favor H. Trott as
captain. “Trott, though a howler, does not expect, as
Giffen does, to get a wicket every ball ; he not only taxes
advice, hut asks for it, whereas Giffen will ignore the
unanimous wish of a whole side. Ami Trott is much
liked by cricketers—an important consideration for a
season’s tour.” Moreover, Trott is a fat man, and every
captain should he fat if it can he so arranged. In picking
the next team for England, the first step will be the ex-
clusion of drunks.
• * • *
King Giffen had decided that did South Aus-
tralia treat England in the return match he would
promptly and easily arrange for taking a purely S.A.
team to London. Even now he has not utterly abandoned
the idea.
* * * *
The Pirates and Universities were in great
fettle for the final Cup contest on Sydney Cricket Ground
last Saturday. Pirates were the favorites, but the
Universities showed the omens of victory two
fat men. Twice the Pirates were pressing up°n the
University line, and twice a University man had tlie
luck and skill to intercept a Pirate pass and dash
away with the Dali, once touching it down himself, and
another time passing it, at the last moment, to another
“ Uni ” who touched it down behind the posts. Then,
too, Nelson, the Pirate captain, kicked a field-goal, the
ball touching a player so slightly in its course that it
deviated not an inch, nevertheless debarring a score.
Pirates, in their eagerness, lost their heads a little, but
the team with the fat men played with cool science,
“Uni’’won by 8 points to 3, and the match is particu-
larly interesting since it proves that not even football is
free from the dire influence of the fat man.
The Challenge Cup has to be won twice in suc-
cession or three times in all, and has been contested for
the last four seasons. This is the order of wins: Rand-
wick, University, Randwick, University.

A. A. Middleton (Vic.) won the Victorian 50-
miles road cycle race last Saturday in 3h. 23m. The first
half was ridden in the teeth of the wind, a condition
which would favor a determined “grafter” like Middleton.
• * * *
Grand Fianenr heads last season’s list of Ans;
traiian winning sires, twelve descendants of the unbeaten
horse having won races, value 19162—Patron and
Patroness's Melb. and Sydney Cups being mainly respnn-
sihle. Trenton is second witli 17 wins, totalling £7613;
and Sheet Anchor is third in the list. Then come
Robinson Crusoe, Nordenfeidt, Splendour, Marvellous,
Chester, Grandmaster, &c., the last-named having the
largest number of winners and victories, 26 of his stock
having pull-d off 66 races. Chester still holds the sires’
record with £17,872 won in 1889 90—a great performance,
considering lie did not “get” that year’s Melb. Cup
winner.

The world’s morality illustrated. A S.A.
publican lately “ sacked ” a barmaid because, in
her joy at winning .£5 on the races, she got drunk.
The old Deutscher, in “ explaining ’’ the matter,
said : “ I minds it not zo mooch ov a girl gits
drunk on vine und help to make de poys shout it;
but ven she get drunk on my own viskey, den I
dinks it vas disgraceful.”
The only known cure for influenza is Cole-
mane and Sons Eucalypte Extract.
“ We landed at Cole’s wharf the day the
Sacramento went ashore. I saw the female
emigrants who had arrived by that ship. When
they landed they entered into conversation with
the men on the wharf, dispensing with the for-
mality of introduction, and showing no maidenly
coyness. At length an emigrant officer came and
marshalled the ladies in processional order, re-
pressed all indecorous levity, and retreated with
his company towards Flinders-street. . • The
descendants of those ladies may be seen in great
numbers, to this day, standing at street corners,
and are called ‘larrikins.’” —From Prairie and
Bush.
Sporting men will be interested to know
that a date has been fixed for the drawing of the
St. Alban’s lottery. The tickets have gone off so
well that Mr. Geo. Adams, who is conducting the
lottery, is confident that the whole 125,000 will be
sold within the next three months, and it has been
definitely decided to hold the drawing on October
24. This will be after the Caulfield Cup, but
before the Y.R.C. meeting, and Mr. W. R.
Wilson’s familiar livery will therefore be absent
from Flemington next November. The distribu-
tion of such a fine lot of rising three-year-olds as
those in the St. Alban’s stable at present may be
expected to slightly unsettle the market for the
next Derby and Cup.*
“You see,’' he said, “my mate and I
were droving, and we had a wild lot of bullocks
always breaking away and giving us no end of
trouble. One night while I was taking my turn,
standing in front of the fire warming my heels and
thinking of home (of course, I had my long dog-
poisoner’s spurs on), away went the mob without
any warning ; so, jumping on the old grey, I sailed
after ’em. But the old horse, the laziest that ever
kicked along, streaked like greased lightning,
regularly galvanised into new life. For a moment
I was downright puzzled, but, happening to look
back, I saw a stream of smoke coming from each
flank, and the mystery was solved. Hang me
if those blanky spurs weren't red-hot. Fact.”
“ Crom-a-boo,” that funniest of Irish comic
football songs, is selling faster than ever.*
The £5-bales and shopping by post, in-
augurated by Riley Bros. 21 years ago, made that
firm’s name a household word not only in N.S.W.,
but throughout Australia, Maoriland and Fiji.
This remarkable success brought out many imita-
tors, who, however, only partially adopted the
original system, sending goods only to railway
towns, whereas Riley Bros, sent the free
to any spot whatever. Once, in the infant days
of the system, the firm paid £2 17s. to send a £ 5-
bale to Mount Brown, which is away in the
extreme N.W. of N.S.W., on the “Burke and
Wills track.” Over 50,000 have already
been sent away; and, knowing perfectly the in-
dustrial and climatic conditions of every district,
and giving more attention than ever to the
bale and shopping by post, and having entirely
revised prices and brought out a new price-list
and illustrated catalogue (3000 useful articles,
with prices), the firm directly enables the remotest
back-blocks resident to take full and cheap ad-
vantage of an enormous and varied stock. The
price-list is sent post free on application ; articles
from Is. upwards will be sent by post; £2 parcels
will be sent free by rail or steamer; the famed
.£5-bale will be sent free to Coolgardie, Carpen-
taria, or anywhere. Although the principal part
of Riley Bros.’ business is in drapery, boots and
shoes, the firm executes any order for saddlery,
harness, ironmongery, tobacco, stationery—in
short, for any part of the stock of a large country
store. Not long ago a whole surveyor’s turn-out
was sent away—horses, carts, waggons, instru-
ments, tents, etc.*
Dinner in bachelors’ quarters on Q’slancl
sugar-plantation. Jaokeroo : “ Tommy, this fella
curry plenty hot; what name curry-powder
belonga him ? ” Tommy (handing a small tin):
“New fella curry-powder; plurry hot him.”
Jackeroo : “ Great Scott! Blank’s insecticide.”
In Fiji, where only Polynesian servants
are employed, the mistress doesn’t think of re-
arranging her dining-room. If you were to torn
a dining-table round the “boy” would bemoan-
able of laying the knives and forks. Y> u woul I
find all the cutlery put on backwards.

“Banjo’s” forthcoming volume will be
entitled “The Man from Snowy Kiver and Other
Verses.” It will bo a handsome book, and the
price will be ss. The original edition (which will
include some verses hitherto unprinted) will con-
sist of one thousand copies only. Post-free from
Bulletin office, ss. 6d.
Confidence is the very backbone of social
and commercial life, and confidence is only begot-
ten by fidelity. These apparent platitudes are
designed to describe the raison d'etre of the
Trustees, Executors, and Agency Company, Ltd.,
,of Melb., whose business announcement appears
elsewhere. The company acts as executor,
administrator, trustee, attorney under power, or
agent for absentees, &c., &c. The main advan-
tages offered to the public are absolute security as
trustee, &0., and economical and efficient repre-
sentation as agent. The company doesn’t die or
leave the country as an individual might. As its
profits are derived as commission it is not exposed
to ordinary risks of trade. Its position can be
ascertained at any time, and it is subject to the
constant supervision of the courts. Also, £lO,OOO
is invested in the name of the Treasurer of Vic-
toria as guarantee for the proper performance of
any duties undertaken. The management is
sound and good, and the company’s very poetical
motto, culled apparently from Tennyson, “ I go
on for ever,” may be regarded as fully justified by
the circumstances of the ease. *
Nervous sufferers will find, ifc worth their
while to peruse ilerr llassmussen’s advt. on page
19 of this issue.

Statistics show that only 25 per cent, of
the successful Girton and Newnhain girls marry.
Hev. Haskett Smith seems to be a staunch
jingo and a friend of the unspeakable Turk. Re
the latest revival of “atrocities in Armenia” (a
very old newspaper drama), the parson is “ not
prepared to state that the alleged atrocities have
no foundation, but he doesn’t doubt that the

Dear Bulletin,Curly Dick” Hunt,
who was knocked off a footboard at Ballarat
cattle-yards, the other day, by one of the beasts
he had slaughtered in thousands, receiving in-
juries which terminated fatally, was, in his
prime, one of the smartest butchers that ever
struck Australia. I never remember being roused
to a higher glow of sporting enthusiasm than by
the bullock-dressing match which took place in
58, 'for .£5O aside, between Dick and William
Mnthner, a young Sydney native. Th« match
came off near the site of the Eureka Stockade,
Walter Craig (of Craig’s Hotel), whose jockey
carried crape on his sleeve when he won the 1870
Melb. Cup with Nimblefoot, being one of the
timekeepers. The bullocks were killed and bled,
and each placed under a rope running through an
overhead block to a ready-manned winch. It was
a treat to watch the men when the word to start
was given. Hunt, with one bound, reached the
operating end of his bullock, slipped his knife
round the neck once, then, taking the instrument
in his teeth, he seized the horns with both hands,
placed his knee against the neck, gave one terrific
wrench, which only required a slash with the
knife to complete decapitation, and the head was
thrown by an outward shove that bumped the
ground and made the spectators hop to miss the
spatter. The same rapid operation was performed
at the joints of the legs, then a plunge with the
knife through the muscles of the hind-legs to
admit the ends of the camel, and, as Hunt gave
the word to hoist, he made two preliminary
slashes from the severed joints to the centre of
the belly, then, getting his knife under the skin,
he made one long upward cut the full length of
the carcase, and had the hide half-off by full-
length strokes before the bullock was swung.
Both men were ambidexterous, and about equal
with the knife; though Hunt made one left-
handed stroke in skinning that sent his arm clean
through the hide. But he showed marked
strength and superiority with the saw and bill-
knife. The match was won by Hunt in 9mins.,
Mutimer finishing Slsecs. later. It was anything
but a good performance for the winner, as he had
frequently done the same thing in Bmins. in prac-
tice.—Old Sport.

Jacky.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
With no child companionship, it was very lonely
for me, out at the station homestead ; so I was
more than delighted when father brought Jacky
home with him, one day, and told me he was to
be my playmate, and that I was to treat him
kindly, just as if he were a little brother.
At first we had to keep the little fellow a
prisoner, until he became acquainted with us, and
with his new surroundings ; but he soon got over
all shyness, and submitted kindly to the trammels
of civilisation. He and I became excellent friends,
and roamed together within the inner enclosure ;
beyond I was not allowed to go without adult
escort.
A victim to bronchitis, I had been a prisoner
in the house nearly all the winter, and our idle
rambles on those lovely spring days are among
my pleasantest memories. We could not con-
verse, as the little native was unable to connect
English words into sentences, although he readily
learned simple words and short phrases. Some-
times of an evening he would snuggle down in a
corner and hold quiet converse with himself in a
melodious pianissimo ; at others, he would whistle
fragments of tunes he had picked up, and, on
these latter occasions, he was always in excellent
spirits, and we would play with the kitten until
bedtime, when Jacky, by his own desire, was let
out for the night—one of his keenest objections
to civilisation being the habit of sleeping in-
doors. At the first peep of dawn he would
waken me by vigorously tapping at my bedroom
window, and we would go down to the bails and
superintend the milking of the cows—an operation
in which Jacky always took the keenest interest.
Although the cuisine of the house seemed to
suit the gastronomic tastes of my playmate, he
retained one objectionable appetite of his race—a
relish for wood-grubs—after a surfeit of which
delicacy he always wended his way to the creek
and “ topped-off ” with a dessert of watercresses.
On several occasions, after these debauches, he
was very ill, and father relieved his sufferings with
generous doses of castor oil. Jacky eventually
regarded these as a portion of the feast, and
signified (by lying on his back, with his legs
drawn up) that there were disturbances in the
interior, and that castor - oil was an urgent
desideratum.
One day we were roaming along the inner
boundary, when Jacky suddenly flitted over the
fence, and darted into the bush. He was off after
grubs. On these occasions he ignored all com-
mands, and I was powerless to detain him. It
was nearly tea-time when he returned, and by
nightfall he was in a sorry plight—lying on his
back and making strange guttural noises. The
usual dose of castor-oil was given him, and he
seemed to have revived by the time I was sent to
bed.
When I awoke in the morning it was bright
sunlight, and father was standing at my bedside
looking very grave. “ Where’s Jacky ?” I asked.
“Bad news, sonny,” said father. I knew in-
stinctively what it was and burst into tears, with-
out waiting to hear more.
How sorrowful were those after-days ! I have
mourned other and greater losses since then ; but
no grief of mine has been more acute or sincere
than was that for the loss of my only playmate.
Whether out of sympathy for me or for the death
of Jacky I cannot say, but both mother and Ellen
(our usually emotionless old cook) were reduced to
tears, and I think father was very near it.
A post-mortem revealed thirteen large, un-
digested grubs and a hard ball of watercress in
Jacky’s crop. We buried him in a candle-box, at
the foot of the big gum-tree, and it was many
weeks before I so far forgot my sorrow as to
neglect watering the flowers which mother had
planted on the little mound.
That was long ago—far away in the bush ; but,
on some days, I wish I could exchange the veran-
dah-posts of Bourke-street for the tall straight
trunks of the gums ; the jostling, impatient crowd
for the gentle, patient sheep; the discordant
street-cries for the hilarious laughter of the
’burra; and the monotonous, unyielding pavement
for the lawn of that station-farm of twenty years
ago.
Melbourne,
WALTER MOSS.

Henniker Heaton strikes oil in North
American Review, claiming that the American
and English nations should take over the Atlantic
cables, of which there are 12. He wants the
charge reduced from Is. a word to Id. There are
23,000,000 messages annually, but the existing
wires could do 283,824,000, on H. H.’s calculation.
Only two of the cables are extensively employed.
Dear Bulletin, — Re paint - suggesting
whisky, here’s a unique experience. Well-mean-
ing person locks up in his room a “tanked” friend,
who fossicks out a pint of methylated spirit, and
therewith solaeeth his captivity to such purpose
that, when he further strikes what he takes to be
another pint of the same, he negotiates that, with-
out discovering that it is—kerosene ! And he
didn’t die ! —wasn’t even sick ! I should know !
—for I am He.
Eugene Robespierre, said to be a grand-
nephew of the “sea-green incorruptible” of the
Terror days, has been hauled up in Paris for
giving short weight in coals. Probably one of
Robespierre’s ancestors was fined for selling bad
butter. The man whose name overshadows the
earth is generally sandwiched in between a genera-
tion which sells bad butter, and another which
gives light-weight in coals.
Arthur Orton’s precious “confession”
brings out all the vanity of the man. He boasts
of his cleverness in answering 9,300 out of 12,000
questions, and attributes his nonsuit in the first
trial to his counsel, Sergeant Ballantyne, whom
he had insulted. The fact was that Ballantyne
asked for a nonsuit when his fat client was hope-
lessly and disgracefully beaten. Coleridge’s cross-
examination had proved Orton to be an absurdly
hollow impostor. The fellow had every oppor-
tunity to change himself into Roger Tichborne
before the trial commenced. The whole uneventful
life of Roger was shown to him. Dozens of en-
thusiastic believers tried to load him up with facts,
but Orton wasted the time that he should have
devoted to Tichborne studies in bloating his
carcase and shooting pigeons.

Excellency Madden, speaking of parsons
Strong and lacker, at Melb. Town Hall, the other
day, said, “They had shown much of the apostle,
but little of the financier. They were abun-
dantly endowed with the spirit of their Master
when He said, ‘Feed the hungry,’ but without
His power of multiplying the loaves and fishes.
Even if the fairly practical clergymen he was com-
plimenting were a couple of ignorant little bethel
bigots, Chief Justice Madden’s endorsement of
the loaves and fishes myth would not have been
excusable. He surely isn’t paid a large salary to
hold up the tattered banner of religious supersti-
tion between the public and the light.
Smoke Eagle Cigarettes.*
The preliminary proceedings in the Jabez
Balfour case have created a widespread opinion
that the oily saint may yet get off. _ The alleged
swindle for which he is being tried is so vast and
so tangled-up that the prosecution seemed to get
lost among the masses of figures; and Jabez, whose
capacity for arithmetic is boundless, had every
one of the transactions of all his companies in his
large, greasy head, and tripped up the Crown
lawyers constantly. He was calm, and fat and
urbane as ever. During the police-court hearing,
he drew a gentleman aside with a soft, moist
hand, to ask how Grace’s score was getting on,
and when he heard that the veteran had made his
100th century, he beamed through his glasses
with a childlike gladness. If Jabez B. is con-
victed, he will go off to gaol with an unctuous
smile on his face, and a look of absolute uncon-
cern ; and he will be a model prisoner, and a com-
fort to all the other inhabitants of the gaol. It is
pretty safe to lay long odds that he won’t collapse,
like Wilde, when the trouble really comes._ lat
and snuffle are proof against almost every kind of
disaster. Fat and cynicism evidently are not.
Smoke Eagle Cigarettes.
Dear Bulletin, —Re your “Ethics of
Oscar” —all conceptions of abstract ethical right-
ness or wrongness must have reference, either to
a postulated inborn criterion, or to a code cate-
gorically prescribed by some so-called “divine
revelation.” But, to those of us who see in ethics
no more than the gradually evolved product
of social expediency, any ethical standard, outside
the “ majority-sense ” of the community, is a mere
figment of the imagination. Might is, for us, not
only, as you say, “practically right,” but also
right absolutely and finally—a principle upon
which the stronger individual would every-
where deal with the weaker, did not the com-
munity, for the sake of its collective welfare,
interfere with a yet superior strength. The
“rights” of the individual, in a word, do not
appertain to him qua individual, but qua unit of
a social totality.—Heretic. [This confuses right
in theory with right in practice. “Practically”
—in practice—“ might is right ”—i.e, might makes
right, sets a conventional standard of right. But
might is not right “ absolutely, finally ” — i.e.,
abstractly and ethically—for thousands of com-
munity-majorities have upheld at various times
differing and contradictory standards of right—
which cannot all be right “ absolutely and finally.”
To say that “might is right absolutely and
finally ” is to find a moral sanction for tyranny,
murder, and outrage—surely a monstrous con-
clusion. As Donisthorpe says, “morality must
be tested by expediency,” but how can it be
shown that the burning of a “ Heretic ” is expe
dient, even from the point of view of humanity ?
And, from the point of view of the “Heretic,”
which has equal ethical right to consideration, it
is probably inexpedient, and therefore immoral.—
Ed. B.]

A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of 660 ft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery ; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornament Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half a million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half a mile
long. *
A Maorilander just returned from a trip
to the States, relates how he went into a barber s
shop in New York for a shampoo, and how he
was induced to try “the egg.” > Now an egg-
shampoo is not what “ colonials ’ are used to;
and when the shampee was passing out he handed
the girl at the counter a ticket, Saidtshe “ Egg-
shampoo— one dollar, please.” Well, said the
Maorilander, “I could understand the|2d. for the
egg, but blow me if I can see now where the four
shillings came in.”

Dray-Dreams.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Oh 1 the mountains speak of sadness,
There is gloom on ridge and spur ;
But they cannot dim the gladness
In my heart because of her :
All day long I feel her near me ;
To my soul her presence steals ;
When I whisper she can hear me
Through the rolling of the wheels.
I can see my gold-haired Freda
(By the dull world undescried)
As I steer the old brown leader
Deftly down the mountain side ;
As I chide the lazy shatter
Through the flower-bespangled dells,
None but I can hear her laughter
Chiming sweet as silver bells.
And at times I hear her singing
Softly when the west winds blow,
And the feathered pines are swinging
On the range-top to and fro :
Dreamily I drive my cattle,
Lulled to sleep above their reins,
By the wheels’ eternal rattle
And the clinking of the chains.
And her songs of love have stirred me,
And I’ve answered from the shaft,
Till the wondering possums heard me
And the kookaburras laughed !
Passion reigns the wide world over;
Prince and pauper own his sway;
And a lover is a lover
Though he drive a two-horse dray !
Days are long and wheat’s to carry
Now, but when the summer’s by
Fate allowing, we shall marry,
Gold-haired Freda, you and I.
When in stress of winter weather
Flowers are dead and skies are gray,
We’ll go jogging home together
Loved and lover in the dray.
Life is dull and toil unending,
But the voice of Love is sweet,
And the tide is always tending
To the tryst where lovers meet;
Life is commonplace and real,
Yet along its rock-strewn way
Each man sees his own ideal
As he dreams upon his dray !
N.S.W.
GLENROWAN.
A subscriber to one of the largest Vic.
circulating libraries asked the librarian if they
kept back files of The Bulletin. “No,” was
the answer. “Then why not?—you tile all the
other papers, and why not The Bulletin ? ”
“ Well, we can’t,” said the librarian, “ too many
people use it; it’s always too ragged when it
leaves the table.”
The Wedding at Our Selection.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Kate was soon to be married. Joe said
that must have been why Sandy Taylor had
come so often and had carried wood for the girls.
We talked a lot about the wedding, and were very
pleased it would be held in our own house, because
all of us could go then. None of us could work
for thinking of it —even Dad seemed to forget
that the corn hadn’t come up and that Mick Bros'
nan was going to summon him for half the fence.
Mother said we would want plenty of water for
the people to drink, so Sandy yoked his horse to
the slide,- and he, Dad and Joe started for the
springs.
The slide was the fork of a tree, alias a wheel-
less water-trolly. The horse was hitched to th e
butt-end, and a batten nailed across the prongs
kept the cask from slipping off going uphill.
Sandy led the way and carried the bucket; Dad
went ahead to clear the track of stones ; and Joe
straddled the cask to keep her steady.
It always took three to work the slide.
The water they brought was a little thick—
Old Anderson had been down and stirred it up
pulling a bullock out; but Dad put plenty ashes
in the cask to clear it.
Each of us had his own work to do. Sandy
knocked the partition down and decorated the
place with boughs; mother and the girls cooked
and covered the walls with newspapers, and Dad
gathered cow-dung and did the floor.
m • •
Two days before the wedding. All of us
were still working hard. Dad was up to his arm-
pits in a bucket of mixture, with a stack of cow-
dung on one side, and a heap of sand and the shovel
on the other. Dan and Joe were burning a cow
that had died just in front of the house, and
Sandy had gone to town for his tweed trousers.
A man in a long, black coat, white collar, and
new leggings, rode up, spoke to Dad and got off.
Dad straightened up and looked awkward, with
his arms hanging wide and the mixture dripping
from them. Mother came out. The cove shook
hands with her, but he didn’t with Dad. They
went inside —not Dad, who washed himself first.
Dan sent Joe to ask Dad who the cove was.
Dad spoke in a whisper and said he was Mr.
Somebody, the clergyman who was to marry Kate
and Sandy. Dan whistled and piled more wood
on the dead cow. Mother came out and called
Dan and Joe. Dan wouldn’t go, but sent Joe.
Dan threw another log on the cow, then thought
he would see what was going on inside.
He stood at the window and looked in. He
couldn’t believe his eyes at first, and put his head
right in. There were Dad, Joe and the lot of
them down on their marrow-bones saying some-
thing after the parson. Dan was glad that he
didn’t go in.
How the parson prayed ! Just when he said
“ lead us not into temptation ” the big kangaroo-
dog slipped in and grabbed all the fresh meat on
the table while their backs were turned ; but Dan
managed to kick him in the ribs at the door. Dad
groaned and seemed very restless.
When the parson had gone Dad said that what
he had read about “reaping the same as you sow”
was all rot, and spoke about the time when we
sowed two bushels of barley in the lower paddook
and got a big stack of rye from it.
# # #
w n
The wedding was on a Wednesday, and at
three o clock in the afternoon. Most of the people
came before dinner; the Harailtons arrived just
after breakfast. Talk of drays! the little paddook
couldn’t hold them
Jim Mullins was’the only one who came in to
dinner ; the others mostly sat on their heels in a
row and waited in the shade of the wire-fence,
The parson was the last to come, and as he passed
ih he knocked his head against the kangaroo-leg
hanging under the verandah. Dad saw it swing-
ing, and said angrily to Joe: “ Didn’t I tell you
to take that down this morning? ”
Joe unhooked it and said : “But if I hang it
anywhere else the dog’ll get it.” _
Dad tried to laugh at Joe and said, loudly:
“And what else is it for ?” Then he bustled Joe
off before he could answer him again.
Joe didnt understand, , , „ n
Ihen Dad said (putting the leg in a bag): Do
you want everyone to know we eat it, you ? ”
Top understood
The ceremony commenced. Those who could
squeeze inside did so-the others looked in at the
window and through the cracks in the chimney.
Mrs, M'Doolan led Kate out of the back-room;
then Sandy rose from the fire-place and stood
beside her. Everyone thought Kate looked very
nice; and orange blossoms ! You’d think she
was an orange-tree with a new bed-curtain thrown
over it. Sandy looked well, too, in his snake-belt
and new tweeds; but he seemed uncomfortable
when the pin that Dave put in the back of his
collar came out.
The parson didn’t take long; and how they
scrambled and tumbled over each other at the
finish ! Charley Mace said that he got the first
kiss ; Big George said he did; and Mrs. M'Doolan
was certain she would have got it only for the
baby.
Fun! there was fun! The room was cleared
and they promenaded for a dance—Sandy and
Kate in the lead. They continued promenading
until one of the well-sinkers called for the con-
certina—of course, we hadn’t one, but Jim Burke
jumped on his horse and went home for his.
Dance! they did dance !—until sun-rise. But
unless you were dancing you couldn’t stay inside,
because the floor broke up, and talk about dust!
—before morning the room was like a drafting-
vard
y '» # *
„ , , , , ~ ~ i j
Sandy had no selection then, so he and
Kate lived with us, and occupied the back-room—
Dan and Dave slept in the front-room, on the
floor. Dad did up the back room for Sandy and
Kate—stuffed rags in all the cracks, and hung
some bagging for a door. It served nearly as well
as a wooden door, and made less noise, though it
didn’t keep little Bill from looking in.
It was a great wedding ; and though years have
since passed, everyone says still it was the best
they were ever at.
Bananaland.
STEELE RUDD.
The Bulletin is now to be read in Yic.
Parliamentary library. Hitherto it has been ex-
eluded as an “ objectionable ” paper which might
corrupt the minds of such saints as Tom Bent A
petition to the library committee asking that it be
placed on the table was taken round by Dr.
Maloney and signed by nearly 80 members.
The lady-students at Melb, Dental College
make astonishingly rapid progress, and some of
them are already much more competent to fill a
tooth than the average male creature who passes
for a qualified dentist.
Malicious and hysterical females will please
note that Alice Brown, of Melb., who recently
told a circumstantial tale regardingabogus outrage
committed upon her, has gotthreemonths’imprison-
ment for perjury. It isn’t much, but it’s a begin-
ning. If a man had perjured himself in (Say) a'
divorce-case and been convicted, he would have
got three years.
A Maoriland widow is suing one Waters
for .£2OO damages sustained through his having
transferred himself to her by marriage contract
when he was not negotiable, as it were, he being
at the time another woman’s lawful property.
Defendant, in trying to disprove damages, cruelly
drew attention to the fact that plaintiff was a
widow.
An association is being formed in Mel-
bourne “ for the Protection of Children.” H. H.
Champion takes a prominent part, and the other
movers are mostly wielders of the holy and insinu-
ating gimlet. Considering that these latter’s last
protegee was proved to be a designing minx of the
deepest dye, a society for the Protection of Lone
Man would seem more in order. Under exist-
ing conditions it certainly is not well for man
to be alone. A timid man can only breathe freely
when in the company of two female witnesses of
good character.
Gray, M.L.A. (Vic.), having stated at a
pious provincial bun-struggle that he knew M.’sP.
in Melbourne who had wives and families in one
suburb and kept a string of mistresses in another,
has been busy denying it ever since and insisting
that the Vie. M.P". is the very pink of propriety
and always goes straight home from church. One
reporter after another, however, has arisen to
confound the good Mr. Gray with his own words,
and the unfortunate statesman is in the unpleasant
position of having told the truth inadvertently or
by mistake. Meanwhile his friends are a sore
trial to him with their imitations of Elton asking
the way to the harem.
The Prince of Wales is again in town on
Sundays, The Bulletin rejoices to hear, there-
fore, H.R.H. has resumed “the delightful Bohe-
mian evenings which his friends delight to arrange
for him, at which the champagne is dry, the cigars
first-rate, the music light, and the ladies —if any
are present—are pretty.” If no ladies be present,
the yarns are beautiful, no doubt. Royal birth,
according to the theosophical Countess Wacht-
meister, is the outcome of a furious ambition for
power in the soul’s previous state. Brood over
ambitious designs, concentrate your thoughts upon
the possibilities of attaining to monarchy, and
half-a-century hence you may have a little mob of
loving subjects loading you up with dry cham-
pagne, choice cigars, ballet music and beauty’s
smiles every calm Sabbath evening that you care
to stop in town.
